o

Introduction
‘‘H OW TH E OTH E R H ALF . . .’’

D

uring the ten years between 1877 and 1887, through which Jacob Riis
was delivering his impassioned lectures concerning New York’s ‘‘invisible’’ poor, photography—which he so 
---
amously championed—reached
a new evidentiary crescendo. How the Other Hal
---
 Lives: Studies among the Tenements o
---
 New York (1890), Riis’s explosive conjunction o
---
 words and images,
has rightly taken a central place among works on the history o
---
 photography, 
---
or it was a 
---
ulcrum o
---
 photography’s collision with politics and li
---
e
and an exemplary case o
---
 the image’s ability to reconﬁgure its re
---
erent. Riis
opened his work with the observation that ‘‘one hal
---
 o
---
 the world does not
know how the other hal
---
 lives’’ (1997 [1890], 5), and he used photography as a
shamanic trace exported 
---
rom one demiworld to the other. The collection
o
---
 essays in this volume was precipitated by the realization that photography itsel
---
 is now in need o
---
 a similar revelation o
---
 its own other hal
---
, its
own disavowed other history.
This volume seeks to change the 
---
ocus o
---
 the critical debate about photographic practice. By abandoning the notion that photographic history is
best seen as the explosion o
---
 a Western technology whose practice has
been molded by singular individuals, Photography’s Other Histories presents
a radically diﬀerent account o
---
 a globally disseminated and locally appropriated medium. In its details o
---
 the signiﬁcance o
---
 colonial photographic practice in the 
---
ormation o
---
 metropolitan sel
---
-identity, the book
also presents case studies o
---
 contemporary photographic sel
---
-
---
ashioning.
Further, it addresses the importance o
---
 photographic records in the his-

torical and autobiographical 
---
ormations that people construct 
---
or themselves, as well as the relationship between photography and other media.
Through various substantive studies, photography’s mimetic doubling becomes a prism through which to consider questions o
---
 cultural and sel
---
identity, historical consciousness, and the nature o
---
 photographic aﬃrmation and revelation.
Just as one might extend the history o
---
 photography 
---
ar back beyond 1839
so as to incorporate entire traditions o
---
 indexical experimentation and ontology, so we might also extend the history o
---
 photography laterally outward to domains outside the purview o
---
 conventional narratives. Within
Buddhism, 
---
or example, there is a complex blurring with ancient ontological expectations, which places photography in a very diﬀerent—and
much longer—chronology than that normally ascribed to it by historians
o
---
 photography: ‘‘The icons o
---
 the Buddha are sometimes compared to the
‘original’ shadow that he is said to have le
---
t in a cave at Nagarahara’’ (Faure
1998, 804). In central Indian popular use, ‘‘photography’’ is a practice that
also incorporates other media such as painting and chromolithography,
and indexicality is a property shared by these diﬀerent media: photos are
not clearly marked as ‘‘modern’’ because their ‘‘
---
unctions are duplicated by
so many other 
---
orms o
---
 palpably ancient representation’’ (Pinney 1997a, 112).
This volume, which has as its genesis an international con
---
erence held
at the Museum o
---
 Queensland in Brisbane in late 1997, is notable 
---
or its
cultural and historical reach. The saliency o
---
 photography to postcolonial
debates is marked herein by the contributions o
---
 two indigenous Australians and o
---
 a practicing Seminole/Muskogee/Diné artist. The Australians,
Driessens and Aird, power
---
ully consider the signiﬁcance o
---
 photographic
traditions in indigenous communities and the relationship between personal memories and that which is encoded in the archive. Much recent
writing that seeks to historically contextualize photography’s emergence
during a period o
---
 colonial expansion has drawn on crucial insights 
---
rom
Edward Said and Michel Foucault and has tended to construct photographic imagery and practice as immovably within a ‘‘truth’’ that simplistically reﬂects a set o
---
 cultural and political dispositions held by the makers
o
---
 those images. Perhaps the starkest o
---
 these contributions is that oﬀered
by the Algerian poet Malek Alloula in The Colonial Harem (1987). By consciously eschewing the study o
---
 the actual political and historical consumption o
---
 images (Barthes’s claim that ‘‘the reading o
---
 public photographs is always at bottom, a private reading’’ appears as an epigraph in

2

Christopher Pinney

Alloula’s book), Alloula spins an eloquent but untested hypothesis concerning the role o
---
 photography as ‘‘the 
---
ertiliser o
---
 the colonial vision
[producing] stereotypes in the manner o
---
 great seabirds producing guano’’
(4). The veil that Algerian women presented to this colonial vision was received as an aﬀront by photographers (‘‘the whiteness o
---
 the veil becomes
the symbolic equivalent o
---
 blindness; a leukoma, a white speck on the eye
o
---
 the photographer and his viewpoint’’ [7]), and a vengeance o
---
 visibility
and nudity was wreaked on this inviolability that so deeply ‘‘haunt[ed] the
photographer-voyeur’’ (13).
Such debates tend to invoke 
---
ormal readings o
---
 images that are then
made to do the work o
---
 a preexisting political hypothesis. In Carlo Ginzburg’s words these are ‘‘physiognomic’’ readings, in which the analyst
‘‘reads into them what he has already learned by other means, or what
he believes he knows, and wants to ‘demonstrate.’’’ Underpinning this approach, Ginzburg continues, is ‘‘the conviction that works o
---
 art, in a broad
sense, 
---
urnish a mine o
---
 ﬁrst hand in
---
ormation that can explicate, without
intermediaries, the mentality and emotive li
---
e o
---
 a distant age’’ (1989, 35).
Ginzburg raises a pro
---
ound methodological issue o
---
 pressing relevance to
all those working with imagery and arti
---
acts, and although this is ultimately
an unresolvable problem, the manner in which many arguments about the
‘‘political eﬀects’’ o
---
 images overlay them with conclusions arrived at by
‘‘other means’’ is especially striking and troubling.
That the 
---
ormal qualities o
---
 images themselves may be in large part irrelevant is suggested by their historical trajectories and the radical revaluations that they undergo. I
---
 an image that appears to do a particular
kind o
---
 work in one episteme is able to per
---
orm radically diﬀerent work
in another, it appears inappropriate to propose inﬂexible links between

---
ormal qualities and eﬀect. Instead, we need a more nuanced reading o
---
 the
aﬃnities between particular discursive 
---
ormations and the image worlds
that parallel them, as well as sophisticated analyses o
---
 their trans
---
ormational potentialities.
What are the consequences, 
---
or instance, o
---
 the documented 
---
act that
‘‘collectors o
---
 North A
---
rican, Near and Middle Eastern descent dominate
the market 
---
or Orientalist art’’ (Benjamin 1997, 32)? Those paintings, which
Said (1978) and Nochlin (1983) have argued projected an image o
---
 largely
negative alterity, are now eagerly consumed by those whose reality these
images so distorted. Roger Benjamin’s researches with those who market
these paintings indicate that a nostalgic invocation o
---
 ‘‘indigenous iden-

tities through images o
---
 the pre-colonial past’’ is involved, together with
‘‘a new sense o
---
 positive empowerment expressed through the acquisition
and thus redeﬁnition o
---

The point here is not to attempt to invalidate Said’s hypothesis—which
remains o
---
 
---
undamental importance to all cross-cultural work—but rather
to raise a set o
---

---
or 
---
urther investigation. A greater sense o
---

the 
---
ragility and instability o
---

---

o

---
emale nudes (1997,
34, 37).

Personal Archives
Jo-Anne Driessens’s and Michael Aird’s contributions to this volume recast the problem o
---
 the colonial archive. By addressing its existence not in
terms o
---
 ontological generality but rather as located in a speciﬁc postcolonial moment, Driessens and Aird reveal the archive’s contents as something like the cargo o
---
 a ship tossed on the waves o
---
 a heavy sea, subject
to movements that produce a rearrangement and recoding. This reconﬁgured archive, inﬂuenced by new demands o
---
 personal recuperation, assumes a radically new role. In both Driessens’s and Aird’s cases the recoding o
---
 images arose 
---
rom their discovery and recognition o
---
 images o
---


---
amily members, stored like a vein o
---
 gold in the archive. Recuperation
takes the 
---
orm o
---
 a homecoming: the naming o
---
 the 
---
ormerly anonymous,
the individuation and recognition o
---
 persons whose work in the archive
had usually been to ‘‘typi
---
y’’—that is, to exempli
---
y some category. Recuperation here is a kind o
---
 particularization, the enclosing in a new space o
---

domesticity and aﬀection o
---
 images 
---
ormerly lost in the public wilderness
o
---
 the archive.
Aird wonder
---
ully captures the creativity o
---
 the engagement o
---
 Aboriginal peoples with the archive as a ‘‘look[ing] past the stereotypical way in
which their relatives and ancestors have been portrayed’’ (my emphasis).
‘‘Looking past’’ suggests a complexity o
---
 perspectival positions or a multi-

4

Christopher Pinney

plicity o
---
 layers that endow photographs with an enormously greater complexity than that which they are usually credited. The photograph ceases
to be a univocal, ﬂat, and uncontestable indexical trace o
---
 what was, and
becomes instead a complexly textured arti
---
act (concealing many diﬀerent depths) inviting the viewer to assume many possible diﬀerent standpoints—both spatial and temporal—in respect to it. But 
---
or Aird only certain types o
---
 images permit this ‘‘looking past,’’ just as only certain 
---
orms
o
---
 Orientalist images appeal in the current market: he describes how an
image o
---
 Andrew Ball ultimately proved wholly resistant to attempts to
displace it 
---
rom the space o
---
 enumeration and humiliation.
Hulleah J. Tsinhnahjinnie ﬁnds a latency in many images o
---
 Native Americans that parallels the ability to ‘‘look past.’’ Thus in E. P. Niblack’s portrait
o
---
 Johnny Kit Elswa (see ﬁg. 8 in her essay) she 
---
eels a recursive texture, at
the point now o
---
 resur
---
acing. The portrait pictures the Haida Gwaii man
with the Bear clan inscribed on his chest and the dog ﬁsh on his arms.
‘‘Tattoos,’’ Tsinhnahjinnie notes, ‘‘went under the skin to survive, encoded
beneath the skin, programmed to resur
---
ace when the time is right. . . . This
is also how I perceive the art o
---
 aboriginal tattoo, latent images.’’ Tsinhnahjinnie proposes a view o
---
 photography very diﬀerent 
---
rom Barthes’s stress
on the ﬂat image’s preservation o
---
 anterior temporal states. For Tsinhnahjinnie, the photograph is more like a message in a bottle, or like a seed: an
object transmitted to the 
---
uture, ready at any moment to burst 
---
orth.
I have noted that Michael Aird drew ﬁrm limits on the potentiality o
---

‘‘looking past.’’ Tsinhnahjinnie, however, per
---
orms a more diﬃcult recoding, which appeals to a more overtly political space o
---
 action. It is an energetic recoding, a recuperation o
---
 an image o
---
 terrible degradation that
less-strenuous analysts might have abandoned 
---
orever to the practices o
---

genocide that made possible the accumulation o
---
 large parts o
---
 the archive.
George Trager’s photograph o
---
 Big Foot lying dead in the snow at Wounded
Knee records an ultimate subjection, but it provokes in Tsinhnahjinnie a
dream. In this dream she ﬂoats, seeing Big Foot as he is in the photograph.
A small girl then traverses the snow, looking into the 
---
aces o
---
 the dead: ‘‘she
walked over to Big Foot, looking into his 
---
ace. She shakes his shoulders,
takes his 
---
rozen hand into her small, warm hand, and helps him to his 
---
eet.
He then brushes the snow oﬀ o
---
 his clothes. She waits patiently with her
hand extended, he then takes her hand and they walk out o
---
 the photograph. This is the dream I recall when I look upon this image o
---
 supposed
hopelessness.’’

Introduction

5

How are we to understand this volatility o
---
 the image? Peirce’s conceptualization o
---
 the photograph as indexical has recently been much invoked
(‘‘I deﬁne an Index as a sign determined by its Dynamic object by virtue
o
---
 being in a real relation to it’’ [1958, 391]). This relationship o
---
 physical
contiguity between image and re
---
erent certainly played a central role in
the truth claims o
---
 the colonial archive: photography was seen to surpass
and eradicate the subjectivity and unreliability o
---
 earlier technologies o
---

representation. Indexicality was thus mobilized as a guarantee o
---
 ﬁxity.
Photography, so Valéry observed, came to underwrite experience and history: ‘‘The mere notion o
---
 photography . . . suggests the simple question:
could such and such a 
---
act, as it is narrated, have been photographed? ’’ (cited in
Trachtenberg 1989, xiii–xiv; see also my essay in this volume).
But the index need not only imply ﬁxity and stability: it can as easily
be used to undermine this very notion. Photography’s exempliﬁcation o
---

Peirce’s index might be recast in terms o
---
 an inevitable randomness within
the image. What in Peirce’s terms are purely iconic images (e.g., paintings,
drawings) are capable o
---
 excluding randomness because they reﬂect only
the imagination and skill o
---
 their creators, and when those qualities are
present in excess they are capable o
---
 driving out the incidental. Photographic technology, however, is 
---
ounded on a paradox: the very capture o
---

light on ﬁlm implies an ineradicable sur
---
eit. I
---
 we think o
---
 the painter’s
imagination and brush as a ﬁlter capable o
---
 complete exclusion, then the
lens o
---
 the camera can never be closed because something extraneous will
always enter into it. No matter how precautionary and punctilious the photographer is in arranging everything that is placed be
---
ore the camera, the
inability o
---
 the lens to discriminate will ensure a substrate or margin o
---
 excess, a subversive code present in every photographic image that makes it
open and available to other readings and uses. Thus we might understand
photography’s indexicality to be the guarantee not o
---
 closure and ﬁxity,
but rather o
---
 multiple sur
---
aces and o
---
 the possibility o
---
 ‘‘looking past.’’ It
is precisely photography’s inability to discriminate, its inability to exclude,
that makes it so textured and so 
---
ertile. Encoded ‘‘beneath photography’s
skin’’ (Tsinhnahjinnie) this excess lies waiting to resur
---
ace.
This volatility can also be seen to reﬂect the ‘‘misrecognition’’ that lies
at the heart o
---
 photography. Once again, Carlo Ginzburg provides a vital
route to understanding this notion. He has described what may appear
to be a paradox o
---
 the completeness o
---
 those Inquisitorial records that
deal with behaviors to which the makers o
---
 those records would have
been especially hostile. Ginzburg agrees with other commentators that
6

Christopher Pinney

most Inquisitorial records are extremely suspect and reveal that the interogees’ answers merely echo the questions posed by their interrogators
(1989, 160). However, the Friulian trials o
---
 the benandanti (Inquisitorial investigations o
---
 suspected witches) stand out: Ginzburg notes that their
‘‘ethnographic value’’ is ‘‘astonishing’’: ‘‘Not only words, but gestures, sudden reactions like blushing, even silences, were recorded with punctilious
accuracy by the notaries o
---
 the Holy Oﬃce’’ (160). Ginzburg uses these
Friulian documents to argue against the suggestion that (
---
ollowing Jakobsen) all reported speech is ‘‘appropriated and remoulded,’’ suggesting instead that ‘‘a conﬂicting cultural reality’’ can ‘‘leak out’’ 
---
rom these encounters. This ‘‘leaking’’ was much more likely where the quoter o
---
 that
reported speech—in this case Inquisitors—misrecognize what is uttered
to them. As Ginzburg says, the historians’ ‘‘task is much easier when the inquisitors did not understand’’ (162–63). He suggests that where there is ‘‘recognition’’ cultural ﬁlters came into play that mediated and modulated evidence to suit the agendas o
---
 the Inquisitors: where there was recognition,
templates and other preexisting schemata were mobilized that appropriated the new experience to old expectations. Misrecognition thus emerges
as productive: ‘‘We can take advantage . . . o
---
 those invaluable cases in which
the lack o
---
 communication on a cultural level between judges and de
---
endants
permitted, rather paradoxically, the emergence o
---
 a real dialogue—in the
Bakhtinian sense o
---
 an unresolved clash o
---
 conﬂicting voices’’ (164; my
emphasis).
The value o
---
 the Friulian transcripts reﬂects the absence o
---
 ‘‘cultural
ﬁlters’’ that permitted the Inquisitors to mediate the evidence in terms
o
---
 well-worn 
---
ormulas. A ‘‘misrecognition’’ o
---
 benandanti knowledge precluded the Inquisitors 
---
rom excluding anything: they didn’t ‘‘know’’ what
to exclude and as a result ended up recording everything.
This is a use
---
ul metaphor 
---
or photography: however hard the photographer tries to exclude, the camera lens always includes. The photographer
can never 
---
ully control the resulting photograph, and it is that lack o
---
 control and the resulting excess that permits recoding, ‘‘resur
---
acing,’’ and ‘‘looking past.’’
This dimension o
---
 ‘‘photography’s other history’’ reﬂects the speciﬁc
nature o
---
 photography as a technology: prephotographic technologies do
not exhibit this inescapably random element. Photographs are necessarily
contrived and reﬂect the culture that produces them, but no photograph
is so success
---
ul that it ﬁlters out the random entirely. This is another
ground on which we might wish to complicate Foucauldian and Saidian
Introduction

7

approaches that presuppose an absolute ﬁt between the image and the
ideological 
---
orces that appear to motivate the image, as well as those approaches that treat photographs merely as art-historical texts betraying
only a grander sweeping aesthetic intentionality.

Visual Economies
A set o
---
 essays grouped together under the title ‘‘Visual Economies’’ 
---
orms
the second section o
---
 this volume. The title is taken 
---
rom Deborah Poole’s
exploration, in her book on Andean photography, o
---
 the inequalities that
characterize representational domains. Eschewing the term ‘‘visual culture’’ 
---
or its supposition o
---
 consensus and homogeneity, Poole advocates a
stress on unequal ﬂows and exchanges: hence ‘‘economy’’ rather than ‘‘culture.’’ She states that economy ‘‘suggests that this organization has as much
to do with social relationships, inequality, and power as with shared meanings and community’’ (1997, 8). Clearly this is necessary as a corrective to
the potentially utopian conclusions suggested by a stress on photography’s
volatility and inﬁnite recodability. We must not lose sight o
---
 the extraordinary circumstances o
---
 inequality (encompassing the range 
---
rom cultural,
political, and economic hierarchy to systematic genocide) that gave rise
to the vast majority o
---
 the images inhabiting the colonial archive. Poole
has another intent: to stress the globality o
---
 image ﬂows that exceeded the
locality that the term ‘‘culture’’ might imply: ‘‘It is relatively easy to imagine the people o
---
 Paris and Peru, 
---
or example, participating in the same
‘economy.’ To imagine or speak o
---
 them as part o
---
 a shared ‘culture’ is considerably more diﬃcult. I use the word ‘economy’ . . . with the intention
o
---
 capturing this sense o
---
 how visual images move across national and cultural boundaries’’ (8).
Roslyn Poignant inserts photographic representations o
---
 abducted indigenous Australians in the context o
---
 a history o
---
 earlier depictions and
questions the ‘‘diﬀerence’’ that most writings on photography attribute to
its technology. Susan Sontag (1979) 
---
amously expounded the metaphorical aﬃnity between photographic practice and ‘‘capture.’’ Here, Poignant
explores photography as an accompaniment to the literal abduction o
---
 a
group o
---
 Queenslanders who were paraded as the living incarnation o
---
 a
set o
---
 Western 
---
antasies o
---
 savagery through nineteenth-century America
and Europe. A 
---
ragment o
---
 an important 
---
orthcoming work, Poignant’s
essay is concerned with the persistence—across diverse technologies—
8

Christopher Pinney

o
---
 discriminatory representation. Tracing enduring tropes o
---
 ‘‘savagery’’

---
rom 1600 onward, Poignant observes the intimate entwining o
---
 discourses
o
---
 conceptual and visual ﬁxity exempliﬁed in the terms ‘‘stereotype’’ and
‘‘cliché’’—both originally used to describe repetitive printing processes.
Poignant also develops a strategy to destabilize this apparent ﬁxity, which
resonates with the ‘‘excess’’ o
---
 photography discussed above. She detects a
disturbance in photographic images akin to Barthes’s notion o
---
 the ‘‘punctum,’’ characterized by what Berger and Mohr (1982, 96) describe as a
‘‘quotation’’ 
---
rom experience. However, across the centuries a continuity
emerges that links the woodblock, through photography, to the digitized
injustices perpetrated by global media.
James Faris traces the ways in which Navajo have been photographically
depicted, and like Poignant he detects enduring structural 
---
eatures that
regulate the manner in which certain people are represented to the archive. What may appear 
---
aulty or objectionable are not ‘‘ﬂaws in an uneducated, unevolved, unenlightened West [rather] they are the necessary
conditions o
---
 existence o
---
 the Navajo to the West.’’ Faris’s model o
---
 knowledge production is much less optimistic than Ginzburg’s, seeing in the
‘‘optical unconscious’’ o
---
 Navajo photographic representation a limited set
o
---
 ossiﬁed permutations rather than a 
---
ecund ﬁeld o

Morris Low’s signiﬁcant contribution to this volume can help destabilize the sense o

investigated through their singularity (attending to its ‘‘dispersed and conﬂicted character’’ [3]), rather than through an appeal to an archetypal monstrous practice. However, Low reveals—in this singularity—a doubling or

In a subtle and sophisticated essay Christopher Wright suggests that the
New Guinea photographs o
---

(cited in Young 1990, 142). The analysis presented here stresses Hula agency
and the photographic image as a record o
---

---

---

Various contributors to this volume address the impact o

Sel
---
-Fashioning and Vernacular Modernism
The third section o

---

Case studies

metropole. These photographic practices appear to be at an ultimate remove

rom the disciplinary

In the relative absence o

---

undamental questions concerning the social and personal space that

photography comes to occupy. Through the ethnographic exploration o
---

---

Photography has been able to per

---

---

mathematical regulation: Heidegger states that ‘‘man brings into play his
unlimited power 
---
or the calculating, planning, and moulding o
---

(135). It was this world within which Euro-American photography largely
operated.
Photography, however, lives in many other cultural worlds as well. These

---

so striking about the images Sprague discusses is their ‘‘materiality’’—their

---

Cutting across all three sections o
---

diversity is a set o
---

o

---

---

studies are, as Wright suggests, ‘‘supple’’—that is, subject to multiple determinations in diﬀerent times and places.
Although the work in this volume is concerned with the diversity and
alterity within photographic practice, it also attempts to deﬁne the speciﬁcity, or the ﬁeld o
---
 determinations, o
---

endeavor includes both the processual character o
---

its historical and cultural variation. Under the inﬂuence o
---
 theorists such
as Sontag and John Tagg, much writing on photography has—in its concern with the ideological eﬀects o
---

---

space that 
---
requently emerges during the process o
---
 picture making. The
concern with the political consequences o
---
 photography has eﬀectively
erased any engagement with its actual practice.
Collectively, the essays in this volume suggest that photography is a
cultural practice with no ﬁxed outcome. The technologically determinist
tradition within writing on photography recently has been redeemed by
predictions about the trans
---
ormative eﬀect o
---
 photographic digitization
which, it is claimed, will dissolve cherished cultural certainties. Photography’s Other Histories re
---
utes such technological histories by revealing long
traditions o
---
 photographic manipulation and concluding that it is cultural
practice that is the true motor o
---

Relating to Photographs

M

y name is Jo-Anne Driessens, I was born in Ipswich (Brisbane) in
1970. I was adopted at two weeks 
---
rom Warilda nursing home

---

Michael Aird

Growing Up with Aborigines

T

here has been a long tradition o
---
 Europeans photographing Aboriginal people, mainly as subjects o
---
 scientiﬁc enquiry. This was going on
as late as the 1930s when some scientists were still documenting what they
thought was the last o
---
 a dying race, or at least a dying way o
---
 li
---
e, and other
pro
---
essional photographers were still attempting to capitalize on images
o
---
 the exotic. These colonial photographs are well known and have been
widely circulated (see, e.g., ﬁg. 1). Perhaps, surprisingly, these photographs
are o
---
ten valued by Aboriginal people despite the criticism they have been
subjected to. By contrast, however, there are other, private, barely known
bodies o
---
 photography 
---
rom the 1930s that portray a diﬀerent image.
The 1930s was a period when an increasing number o
---
 Aboriginal people
started to become involved with photography by choice. In the ﬁrst 
---
ew
decades o
---
 this century very 
---
ew Aboriginal people were well-oﬀ enough
to be able to aﬀord to visit studios as paying customers. Yet by the 1930s a
growing number began to own cameras 
---
or the ﬁrst time and could aﬀord
to have photographs developed. These kinds o
---
 photographs, o
---
ten held
in Aboriginal 
---
amily collections, are poorly known, as are private 
---
amily
collections taken by non-Aboriginal people living in Aboriginal communities. This essay will examine these various photographic records 
---
rom
the 1930s, in particular those relating to the Aboriginal people o
---
 southeast
Queensland.
Photographs taken by pro
---
essional photographers or government oﬃcials have proved to be extremely valuable to Aboriginal people as historical documents, even though they are o
---
ten heavily inﬂuenced by the
artistic or political inclinations o
---
 the photographer and by misguided and

1. Man and woman, with dead kangaroo, inside a photo studio. Gra
---
ton c.1873.
Photograph by J. W. Lindt. Courtesy o
---
 the Queensland Museum (eh.15).

racist belie
---
s resulting in the portrayal o
---
 Aboriginal people as savages, beggars, or as the last o
---
 a dying race.
I have, however, o
---
ten seen Aboriginal people look past the stereotypical
way in which their relatives and ancestors have been portrayed, because
they are just happy to be able to see photographs o
---
 people who play a part
in their 
---
amily’s history. I have watched as a woman viewed photographs
taken in the 1890s o
---
 her grandmother posing bare-breasted in a photographic studio. This image was in contrast to the way the woman remembered her grandmother, a woman who was always 
---
ully clothed. Yet she
seemed undisturbed by the uncharacteristic way in which the photographer portrayed her grandmother and was simply grate
---
ul 
---
or the opportunity to view a photograph o
---
 her taken so long ago. A
---
ter working with
photographic collections 
---
or over ﬁ
---
teen years, I mysel
---

experience, as well as share with numerous Aboriginal people, the joy and
excitement o
---
 ﬁnding images o
---
 ancestors and relatives.
In 1991 I organized an exhibition called Portraits o
---
 Our Elders, which covered the time in the nineteenth century when Aboriginal people had little
control over the way in which they were portrayed by photographers,
through to the time when some o
---
 them had 
---
ull control over how they
were seen. These latter photographs showed a side o
---
 Aboriginal li
---
e in the
1920s that was ignored in publicly circulated photographs which almost exclusively depicted people in situations o
---
 poverty. The images were taken
inside photo studios and show well-dressed and conﬁdent Aboriginal people who sent their children to schools to be educated in order to improve
their credibility or acceptability within the European society. They were
photographed wearing expensive clothes, and they paid 
---
or copies o
---
 the
photographs 
---
or display in their homes and to give away to relatives.
There are numerous examples o
---
 how Aboriginal people attempted to
become part o
---
 European society, such as ﬁnding employment in various industries, sending their children to schools, becoming Christians,
and settling in urban areas. These can all be interpreted as tactics o
---
 survival, and photography was used to document this process. Survival to Aboriginal people around the turn o
---
 the century, particularly in southeast
Queensland, was not just to go out into the bush and hunt kangaroos but
to survive among what was happening at that time in their land. They
were determined to stay in their traditional country, and every aspect o
---

their lives became part o
---
 a strategy to survive: they had to con
---
orm. These
people were in one sense being Aboriginal, but in another sense they were
attempting to be ‘‘European’’ to survive. The later images in the exhibiGrowing Up with Aborigines

25

tion represent how some Aboriginal people o
---
 southeast Queensland had
gained control over many aspects o
---
 their lives, but un
---
ortunately it was
not the case 
---
or most Aboriginal people at that time.
The photographs 
---
eatured in Portraits o
---
 Our Elders demonstrate the contrast o
---
 Aboriginal people being portrayed as ‘‘exotic’’ or as the ‘‘noble savage’’ to very conﬁdent Aboriginal people in poses o
---

particular, the photograph o
---

idea 
---

o

together a series o

One o

Not all photographers have set out with racist motivations to document Aboriginal people. Numerous photographic collections have been
made by European people who have had close associations with Aboriginal
people, such as clergy, police, health workers, schoolteachers, and government oﬃcials who have spent time with Aboriginal communities. Over
the past 
---
ew years there have been a growing number o
---
 photographic
collections being donated to museums and libraries by these people or
by their 
---
amilies. These collections are proving very popular among Aboriginal people, who are now coming into museums and other institutions
searching 
---
or photographs o
---
 their relatives. These photographs are important historical documents not just because they document Aboriginal
people and their daily lives but, more importantly, because they document
relationships between Aboriginal and non-Aboriginal people.
30

Michael Aird

In 1924 Enid Elizabeth Semple, aged eight years, arrived at Barambah Aboriginal settlement (or Cherbourg, as it is now known) as the daughter o
---

government oﬃcials. Her 
---
ather, William Porteous Semple, had been appointed superintendent, and her mother took the position o
---
 matron o
---

the settlement (ﬁg. 7). Enid Semple, now in her eighties and known to most
as Betty McKenzie, remembers back to what she calls a wonder
---
ul childhood growing up with Aborigines.
Betty McKenzie put together an important set o
---

ﬁgs. 8–10). Many o
---

---

---
amily; others were taken by visitors to the settlement such as press or
government oﬃcials who would o
---
ten send photographs o
---

Betty’s 
---
amily. Betty’s collection is important not only 
---
or the photos, but
also 
---
or her memories o
---

---
ormation she attaches to these photographs that makes them an important
historical record.
Betty lived through a very important political time 
---
or Aboriginal people.
However, she does not make an issue o
---

by, but instead chooses to 
---
ocus on the happy memories o
---
 her childhood.
Betty represents the caring and well-meaning attitudes o
---

have Aboriginal children as playmates. But the other white oﬃcials’ children didn’t play with Aboriginal children like I did. The little girls would
come down to play with me, or say ‘what are we going to do today?’ At mid
day they went home to the dormitory 
---
or lunch and I went into our dining room 
---
or a very diﬀerent lunch. They probably had damper and jam,
they had good meals but very plain, while I might have had ﬁllet steak or
whatever’’ (interview with author, 1997).
In 1972 Betty McKenzie gave a presidential address to the Queensland
Naturalists Club titled ‘‘Growing Up with Aborigines.’’ The title o
---

---
 attitudes 
---
rom a past era, but what is important about her address, as well as her photograph collection, is that they
document the diversity o
---

o
---

sadder things, it’s a happy memory. It isn’t a considered memory o
---
 how the
settlement was run, it isn’t oﬃcial, it isn’t oﬃcial at all. It is not what I know
now’’ (interview with author, 1997). Betty did not set out purely to document Aboriginal culture, she simply wanted to document aspects o
---
 her

amily, 
---

---

o

When Is a Photograph Worth
a Thousand Words?

S

tories are always told 
---
rom all corners o
---
 the world: stories o
---
 creation,
stories o
---
 the ethereal, stories o
---
 survival; grandmothers, grand
---
athers
telling stories that were told to them by their grandmothers and grand
---
athers; aunties relating stories about brothers and sisters. We are all 
---
amiliar with the warmth o
---
 stories cradling, surrounding, supporting new generations.
I remember several o
---
 the stories related to me by my mother; as she
spoke I would visualize the scenes in my young mind—just like a television, just like a photograph.
Our 
---
amily photographs 
---
rom the past are very 
---
ew, my mother’s 
---
amily
(Seminole and Muskogee) had a collection o
---
 
---
amily photographs that perished in a house ﬁre during the 1940s. I would occasionally overhear my
mother and Aunt Marie lamenting over the loss. Although I know them

---
rom the beauti
---
ully woven stories, I have never viewed their likeness in
a photograph. My mother’s 
---
ather passed on be
---
ore I was born. I have
never seen an image o
---
 my grand
---
ather. But in my mind—my imagined
photographs—the men are strong and handsome, the women strong and
breathtaking, with lustrous warm dark skin, lightening-sharp witty eyes,
and smiles that could carry one 
---
or days. A photographic album 
---
ull o
---

beauti
---
ul brown people, a photographic album o
---
 visual aﬃrmation.
My 
---
ather’s 
---
amily (Diné) never had very many photographs, there was
no 
---
urious ﬁre to melt the negatives, there was a philosophy which was
very protective. To outsiders I suppose the attitude would be interpreted

as superstitious or even shy. Whatever the outsider pre
---
erred to believe,
whatever the sophisticated evaluation arrived at, the outsider would leave
the reservation satisﬁed that stereotypes had been aﬃrmed. They never interpreted the ‘‘backward attitude’’ o
---
 the subject or shyness as a statement
about their presence. The superstition or shyness was neither explained
nor elaborated on to strangers, because the ‘‘photographer’’ would not have
understood the nuances o
---
 privacy that the Diné perceived.
My Seminole, Muskogee, and Diné relatives may not have shared the
same views about photography, but as American history would have it they
did endure the same government policies created to destroy the very 
---
abric o
---
 Native culture. All three Nations experienced 
---
orced removal 
---
rom
ancestral homelands.
My Seminole and Muskogee relatives were 
---
orced to walk 
---
rom Florida
to Oklahoma, a 
---
orced march that began in the late 1830s and is known as
the Trail o
---
 Tears. My Diné relatives also have a name 
---
or their 
---
orced removal: ‘‘The Long Walk’’ (1867). The 
---
orced marches were in violation o
---

every basic human right imaginable.
The 
---
ocus o
---
 my relatives was the reality o
---
 survival, keeping one’s 
---
amily
alive. Time to contemplate Western philosophy or the invention o
---
 photography was, shall we say, limited. Because o
---
 the preoccupation with survival, Native people became the subject rather than the observer. The subject o
---
 judgmental images as viewed by the 
---
oreigner—images worth a
thousand words. As long as the words were in English.
When I ﬁrst began reading ethnographic images I would become extremely depressed, but then recognition dawned. I was viewing the images
as an observer, not as the observed. My analytical eye matured, and I became suspicious o
---
 the awkward, sel
---
-appointed ‘‘expert’’ narrative. From
delegation photographers, expedition photographers, and ethnographic
researchers, I was very cognizant o
---
 methodologies that were o
---
 the ‘‘objective’’ 
---
oreign eye. But even so ﬂawed, these nineteenth-century images
were very signiﬁcant in ﬁlling the empty pages o
---
 my 
---
amily album.
It was a beauti
---
ul day when the scales 
---
ell 
---
rom my eyes and I ﬁrst encountered photographic sovereignty. A beauti
---
ul day when I decided that
I would take responsibility to reinterpret images o
---
 Native peoples. My
mind was ready, primed with stories o
---
 resistance and resilience, stories o
---

survival. My views o
---
 these images are aboriginally based—an indigenous
perspective—not a scientiﬁc godly order but philosophically Native.
The understanding o
---
 indigenous continuance must be the understanding o
---
 indigenous religion. From healers to message receivers, enduring
When Is a Photograph Worth a Thousand Words?

the past and the continuous assault by Christianity, Native religion and
philosophy hid to survive and resur
---
ace at appropriate moments.
As I look into the eyes o
---

---

ormer principal chie

---

---
ul. . . . How do you know o
---

Adjusting my lens I replied, ‘‘I saw his photograph in a book o
---

---

---
ormation, just a sliver o

told me about Ayyuini and his understudies, and how no one since has
equaled his presence and power. The arrival o
---

---

---
eeling

When I gaze upon the image o

---

vanishing race, I see the contrary, I see perseverance.
In these photos I immediately recognize the power o
---
 survival, and my
heart is ﬁlled with emotion. There is the synthesising o
---
 my existence, the
very reason why this indigenous woman, typing on a laptop computer at
the end o
---
 the twentieth century, exists. The persistence o
---
 that same religion lives within me, ensuring Native survival and thus re
---
using to surrender the soul. Native land may be taken by 
---
orce or by invented written declarations, and natural resources sucked up by an in
---
antile America, but no
matter how many words are written on a piece o
---

---

---
oreign
names, America will always be Native land.
Native people, photographed dramatically in appropriate savage attire,
vanishing be
---
ore one’s eyes. Native people photographed in suits o
---

---
 a progressive new world.
Such schizophrenia lamented the disappearing o
---

celebrated images o
---

FROM P. T. B A RN U M (1883 ) TO THE
SU N DA Y TIMES (199 8 )

The Narrative

I

n October 1993, news ﬂashed round the world that the mummiﬁed body
o
---
 a North Queensland Aborigine, called Tambo, had been 
---
ound in
the basement o
---
 a recently closed 
---
uneral home in Cleveland, Ohio. This
macabre event brought to public attention the little-known story o
---
 the
removal abroad o
---
 Tambo and his companions, nine Aborigines 
---
rom the
Palm Islands and nearby Hinchinbrook Island, all o
---
 whom were probably
picked up in Townsville by the showman R. A. Cunningham, who was acting as recruiter 
---
or the American circus impresario P. T. Barnum. During the circus season o
---
 1883, the Aborigines were 
---
eatured in Barnum’s
‘‘Ethnological Congress o
---
 Strange and Savage Tribes,’’ together with the
Zulu, Nubians, Toda, and Sioux per
---
ormers who had been similarly dispossessed on other colonial 
---
rontiers. A
---
ter the season, through the winter
o
---
 1883–1884, Cunningham and the Aborigines toured the dime museums
o
---
 the northern United States until, in Cleveland, Tambo was the ﬁrst to
die—only a year a
---
ter leaving Sydney. Unknown to his companions, he was
subjected to the ultimate indignity: his embalmed body was put on show
in Drew’s Dime Museum. (It remained on display there and elsewhere in
Cleveland well into the twentieth century.)
It was part o
---
 Cunningham’s pattern to move the group on whenever
there was an incident, and sometime between leaving Cleveland and ar-

riving in London a 
---
ew weeks later another young man died. For several
years, Cunningham continued to tour the seven survivors through the
showplaces o
---
 Europe, their numbers steadily reduced by death. On their
travels, in addition to their show appearances, they were examined, measured, and photographed by anthropologists. Early in my investigations o
---

the Aborigine show group I wondered i
---
 it would be possible to recover
their story. Reasoning that the group may have been regarded as British
subjects, I searched the register o
---
 ‘‘British Deaths Abroad.’’ There, at a
likely date, I 
---
ound the single entry ‘‘Jimmy.’’ It was a long shot, but I paid
my £5 and sent away 
---
or the certiﬁcate. With it came conﬁrmation: Jimmy
had died in Darmstadt, Germany, on 31 May 1885, and his occupation was
given as ‘‘Australian Savage’’ in the Cunningham Company. It was never
again to be as easy to recover what Americans call ‘‘vital records.’’ The manner in which the unwilling travelers were represented in both scientiﬁc
and popular accounts (the sources on which this outline draws) served
to obscure their individual identities, so that deconstructing the linked
graphic, photographic, and textual representations associated with their
per
---
ormance and display has been crucial to the recovery o
---
 their particular histories, however 
---
ragmentary.1 In any event, my main concern here is
with the visual.
Aboriginal lives were made captive, not only in the sense o
---
 loss o
---

agency but also within late-nineteenth-century discourses that linked
the colonial 
---
rontiers to the metropolitan centers o
---
 North America and
Europe. Together with other indigenous per
---
ormers, they became enmeshed in Western systems o
---
 popular entertainment and education involving display and per
---
ormance, which marked the emergence o
---
 the
modern world as spectacle. I call the arena where this engagement took
place the ‘‘show-space.’’ This term is more than a collective name 
---
or the
actual places o
---
 exhibition. Rather, it is a cultural space that, among other
things, serves as a zone o
---
 displacement 
---
or the indigenous per
---
ormers
and a place o
---
 spectacle 
---
or the onlookers—that is, a chronotopic space
(Bakhtin 1981, 84, 250–53) in which historically speciﬁc relations o
---
 power
between colonizers and colonized are made visible. Within this space
the personal stories o
---
 these people intersected with another narrative
concerning the links between popular culture and anthropology. The language lists, bodily measurements, and photographs o
---
 the physical anthropological accounts o
---
 Cunningham’s group were part o
---
 a classiﬁcatory
project that postulated a hierarchy o
---
 races in which Australian Aborigines and other hunter-gatherers were rated low in the scale. Such dubi56

Roslyn Poignant

ous social-evolutionary notions provided the ideological underpinning 
---
or
their dispossession. At the same time, they provided the rationale 
---
or the
treatment o
---
 indigenous per
---
ormers as expendable.2
Although recovering narrative has been central to my research, a parallel
concern (and a main concern in this article) is to investigate the representational processes—what Homi Bhabha (1983) calls ‘‘the processes o
---
 subjectiﬁcation’’—that ‘‘made’’ Tambo and his companions into pro
---
essional savages. My investigation is complicated by the public exposure the story has
received since Tambo’s repatriation and his burial on Palm Island in February 1994, 110 years a
---
ter his death. The historical photographs in particular
have been deployed in the several diﬀerent but interrelated narratives o
---

the descendants o
---
 other Palm Islanders, and o
---
 non-Aboriginal commentators and public alike.3 Although some o
---
 these new narratives are part o
---

the process o
---
 cultural renewal taking place in the community since the
‘‘return’’ o
---
 Tambo, much o
---
 the reporting about these events comes close
to reanimating the sensational and derogatory language o
---
 nineteenthcentury circus literature. Thus the shi
---
ting ground o
---
 the present 
---
osters
both the emergence o
---
 new narratives o
---
 identity and the persistence o
---

old stereotypes—albeit in new digitized clothing. Re-presentation is a relentless process. I propose here to interrogate both old and new representational 
---
orms, concluding with a particular example 
---
rom an article about
present-day Palm Island in the Sunday Times supplement, London (1998), in
which one o
---
 the historical photographs—a portrait o
---
 Jimmy—was digitally manipulated to represent not Jimmy himsel
---
, but the ghost o
---
 Tambo.
Let me begin with two representations, one photographic and the other
graphic, o
---
 Tambo and his companions. The photograph o
---
 the group in
their cowhide show costumes, taken be
---
ore Tambo and another young man
died, is the only one known showing all nine group members (see ﬁg. 1).
Although the photo was almost certainly taken in the United States,4 Cunningham registered its copyright in London on 28 April 1884, giving the
photographer’s address as the same as his own temporary address. The implied London location is an impossibility, given that by April 1884 two
o
---
 the men were already dead; that a copy o
---
 the photo has survived in
the British copyright ﬁles can be attributed to Cunningham’s deviousness.
Although the Aboriginal names o
---
 each group member were recorded
both by Cunningham (in his pamphlet) and several European anthropologists, all except one—the second young man to die—have come to be
known by their given English names. In an essay elsewhere (Poignant 1997)
I have shown that by relating this photograph to other named photographs

In my larger project, ‘‘Captive Lives,’’ on which this paper draws, I am
concerned with what W. J. T. Mitchell calls ‘‘the whole ﬁeld o
---

---

Tambo and his companions. I am concerned with per
---
ormance and display
and with textual and pictorial representations—both graphic and photographic—that enable the excavation o

o
---

papers, as well as anthropological reports. I am concerned with the interlayering o
---

---

---

---
 the 
---
actual and ﬁctional in images, scientiﬁc and oﬃcial reports,
registers and contracts, and 
---
rontiersmen’s tales and romantic novels. At
the same time, I place some emphasis on the visual, because I regard visual
documents—and oral when available—as important in the ‘‘making’’ o
---

social and cultural histories contrary to received histories. Indeed, the
image-rich nature o
---

---

---
orm o
---

deconstructive processes that unlock meaning and constructive processes
o
---

---
ormulation. Although 
---
or the period under discussion it is diﬃcult
to recover, even partially, aspects o

take visual and written

---

---

Encounter
The conventions o
---

have deep roots in earlier representations o
---

---

America (c.1600) provides an early example o

in the bay with landing cra
---
t drawn up on an exotic shore. The meeting
is between allegorized ﬁgures o
---

---

By the late eighteenth century the recurring visual elements associated
with ‘‘encounter’’ had been turned to diﬀerent ideological usages. At the
time o
---

dated 1764 (which Hugh Honour describes as the ﬁrst

Lamenting relatives stand on the shore as a small cra
---

---

---

---
ricans
and shipwrecked Europeans. Widely disseminated as an engraving (by J. R.
Smith), it was meant to complement Moreland’s earlier Execrable Human
Traﬃck. Both paintings were inspired by an antislavery poem (Honour 1989;
ﬁgs. 15, 20, 52–53, 66–72).

In thinking about these developments it is important to recognize that

At the Circus
In America, the expansion o
---

in
---
ormational systems—underpinned most other developments. Take, 
---
or

---

---

From the 1870s, P. T. Barnum led the way in developing the traveling circus as an industrialized 
---
orm o
---
 mass entertainment. His stated target
audience was the middle classes and their respectable working-class allies
(Adams 1997, 90). He and his partner, William Coup, made use o
---

---

---

reduced the time lost between showplaces. The success o
---

---

o
---

---

---
ten in the course o
---

single day. In the 1880s Barnum’s circus used up to three advance publicity
carriages, which were movable workshops as well as transporters. Illustrated newspaper advertisements, a promotional publication called the Advance Courier, posters, and billboards summoned the crowds 
---
rom the surrounding districts, o
---
ten 
---
rom up to 
---
orty miles away, and excursion trains
brought them in (Flint 1977, 1983; Parkinson n.d.).25 Be
---
ore the movies, the
circus was probably the most inﬂuential instrument o
---

shaping public attitudes,26 through an extraordinary range o
---

Barnum’s involvement with the display o
---

---
or commercial

(Reiss 2001). From 1850, Barnum continued to display ‘‘living curiosities’’

In Europe, Cunningham toured the group widely

---

---
or ethnographic displays
was o
---

---
or the mass-circulation press (1982, 125–51).

James Faris

Navajo and Photography

A

s exemplar o
---
 the photographic practices on Native Americans, outlined here are what might be called ‘‘photographic registers in the
imaging o
---
 Navajo’’ (see table 1). This in
---
ormation cannot, o
---
 course, be
claimed to cover all imaging practices on all Native Americans, but what
is noted here is quite likely typical in essential 
---
eatures o
---
 much o
---
 the
experience o
---
 many indigenous people to Western photographers. These
registers are not argued as stemming 
---
rom any single locus, and there is no
agency or extradiscursive site o
---
 privilege 
---
or such an outline. This is not
a map that is exhaustive. In addition, some o
---
 the unique experiences o
---

Navajo are noted.
Navajo people o
---
 the U.S. Southwest (mostly in the states o
---
 New Mexico
and Arizona, and some in Utah) are today the largest pre-Columbian group
north o
---
 Mexico. They number some quarter o
---
 a million people, who
live principally in an area (a reservation established by military 
---
orce and
treaty) o
---
 some twenty-
---
our thousand square miles. This area is largely
high desert and mountains, and it was le
---
t to Navajo because European
Americans at the time considered it largely useless. Navajo resistance to
European American expansion resulted in punitive measures, and in the
mid-nineteenth century several thousand Navajo (probably about hal
---
 the
total population o
---
 the time) were 
---
orcibly rounded up and marched some
seven hundred miles to an incarceration site in an attempt to settle them.
This project 
---
ailed (not because it was judged wrong or immoral but because it proved to be ﬁnancially nonviable) and those remaining alive were
allowed (or 
---
orced) to return to Navajoland some 
---
our years later (1868).
This incarceration and the westward push o
---
 expanding capitalism also co-

Table 1. Registers in the photography o
---
 Navajo.
Surveillance

1. Documentary (oﬃcial, unoﬃcial, advocacy)
2. Anthropological (asset in communicating anthropology’s view,
archive, racial, museum)
3. Casual (early tourist, traveler, worker in area, gaze)

Humanist

1. Sentimental (vanishing race, lost culture, 
---
amily o
---
 man)
2. Victims (dead, dying, non
---
unctional or mis
---
unctional)

Commercial

1. Aestheticist (color/silver/weaving/herding/turquoise, 
---
ashion,
body parts)
2. Landscape (extension o
---

incided with and was accompanied by the earliest photography o

—signiﬁcantly, as captives.
Prior to the arrival o
---

rapidly adopted horses and livestock on the introduction o
---

to the New World. On 
---

---

Anthropology and Western science claim that Navajo are very late
comers 
---
rom the 
---
ar north to their present homeland; indeed, arriving
just in time to greet the ﬁrst Europeans coming north 
---
rom Mexico in
the sixteenth century. Navajo, o
---

---

this major contradiction, a 
---

as autochthonous in their dramatic landscape. Nevertheless, photography,
normally opaque to any time except the 
---

---

---
or

James Faris

tory and their indigenous claims. In a curious perversity, Western ‘‘reality’’
is imaged, not Navajo.

Photographic Registers in the Imaging o
---
 Navajo
Rhetorical and subjective working classiﬁcations may be established 
---
or
the major categories o
---
 Western photographs o
---
 Navajo to the present.
They constitute a catalog o
---
 the lenses through which Navajo are viewed,
and the Western values attached to images o
---
 this American people. These
classes guide photographers and are in turn guided by and bear on established discourses o
---
 conquest and racism.
I am not talking here about intention or motivation—which are not
relevant in any case. These categories, these registers, then, are not based
on consciousness or announced desire, 
---
or all photographs are subjects o
---

ambition and desire. Thus, on the one hand, consciousness and themes o
---

assimilation, adaptation, and pastiche, and, on the other, preservation, nostalgia, and pastoralism, are not classes o
---
 this catalog and are present in
almost every photograph. It could hardly be otherwise.
This classiﬁcation is in
---
ormed by a political interrogation or objection to
the West’s imaging o
---
 Navajo. Judgments are political and subjective. The
valence, which I argue adheres to these photographs, are not ﬂaws in an
uneducated, unevolved, unenlightened West. They are the necessary conditions o
---
 existence o
---
 Navajo to the West, exemplars and important 
---
eatures o
---
 its relationship with Navajo. They are the imaging devices 
---
or the
maintenance o
---
 a speciﬁc and shaped diﬀerence—they can be thought o
---

as an ‘‘optical unconsciousness’’ (Benjamin 1968). They saturate practically
all ways Navajo can currently appear to the West. This scheme is another
Western template—another ordering, normalizing catalog, and one more
project by outsiders on images o
---
 local people made by outsiders. And as
such, it cannot claim to be in
---
ormed with Navajo consciousness. It is not
intended to be. And, readers may notice, serious alternatives, whether by
local people or outsiders, are placed somewhat separately and outside this
scheme. Ever more o
---
 these are now beginning to appear, and this scheme
will become increasingly historical. Nevertheless, it still applies, as o
---
 this
writing, to most Western photographic projects on Navajo.
O
---
 course these classes merge, blend, and mix. But they do establish
something o
---
 the limits and boundaries o
---
 the way Navajo have appeared
or can appear to the West. Obviously this suggests that there are but a
Navajo and Photography

87

ﬁnite series o
---

these photographs are guided by a series o
---

photographs). These are o
---
ten discourses premised on the hierarchies o
---

civilized/savage, sedentary/nomadic, modern/traditional, anthropological knowledge/local ideology, and West/Other. Yet occasionally there is a
truly grand photograph within this registry (see ﬁg. 7). I pre
---
er to think o
---

these somewhat as ‘‘simply luck,’’ as has cultural critic Lucy Lippard (1992).
And this classiﬁcation, as noted in discussing conscious intent, also ignores
sets, collections, and series as particular ordering entities. The U.S. government,

promote its own projects, agendas, and views o

many aesthetic—that is, beauti

Quite obviously other photographs are possible. And they exist. They

None o

---
or almost any minority people. But
this presentation is based on a rather exhaustive examination o
---
 the archival and published illustrations o
---
 one Native American people 
---
rom the
inauguration o

Other Views o
---
 Existing Photographs, and Alternatives
The deliberate destruction o
---
 the existing photographs and ﬁlmic materials o
---
 Navajo subjects is an alternative that has been requested, although
presumably it is not really ever under serious consideration. Photographs
and ﬁlms o
---
 sacred materials, o
---
 the dead and dying, are argued to have
been very dangerous to those involved, and there have been requests that
such images be returned to Navajoland to be destroyed. Such queries have
never, however, amounted to a 
---
ormal request by the higher Navajo authorities who might have suﬃcient status or power to make such a demand, so archivists need not worry. It is an expression o
---
 local people who

88

James Faris

remember the history o
---
 the photographic and ﬁlmic events and know the
consequences.
Readers will have heard these stories because they are legion. Yet there is
such an evidential or aesthetic or historical or some other value attached
to these images by the West, that their return to and/or destruction by
the photographic subjects is considered idiocy or some type o
---
 Luddite irrationality—or not, ultimately, in the best interests o
---
 the people making
the request. A
---
ter all, it is usually argued that archives and museums have
a responsibility to the wider society and only Western history is important. It is the view here, however, that such requests ought to be treated
care
---
ully and seriously. Navajo 
---
requently consider untoward photographs
(those that they did not agree to have made, or over whose distribution
they have had no control) to constitute a danger to them by their very
existence, whether or not publicly available or published, and numerous
lawsuits have been ﬁled based on such objections. They have not, however, been success
---
ul, as the torts governing photographic access in the
United States are not premised on any sorts o
---
 non-Western cultural sensitivities but on issues o
---
 privacy. Indeed, it has been adjudicated that persons o
---
 ‘‘reasonable sensitivity’’ cannot object to having their photographic
image made in public places (see Moreland 1991). That the notion o
---
 ‘‘reasonable sensitivity’’ is exclusively bound to European-American cultural
discourses, is not, o
---
 course, acknowledged. Navajo culture, in such legal
tests, has been judged not ‘‘reasonably sensible.’’
O
---
 course there are other alternatives, such as many under consideration in this volume; 
---
or example, the rereading o
---
 photographs by new
viewers, the admission o
---

the new readings and new uses by local people. And there is particularly
innovative work by local photographers. There have also been a 
---
ew antiexhibitions and reexhibitions. This, however, is usually such a threat to
existing museums o
---

t shops

amount o
---

that despite occasional conversations, such exhibitions are seldom 
---
orthcoming. These antiexhibitions are regarded as holding history hostage to
the present or as promoting dissention and as unwelcome introductions
o
---

---
orgotten. The West has never been generous to exposures
o
---

---

Navajo and Photography

97

Conclusions
Susan Stewart has argued that ‘‘the liberation o
---
 representation into a domain o
---
 signiﬁcation always opens onto a pattern or structure o
---
 absences
—that which is the task o
---
 the representation to bring 
---
orward as a ‘making
present’ ’’ (1991, 6). This essay has argued that the visual and discursive space
available in most Native American photography to date (and particularly

---
or photography o
---
 Navajo) has been very narrow and o
---
 remarkably 
---
ew
registers. It has been characterized by what Stewart calls ‘‘a structure o
---

absences.’’ The available tropes appeared early and have been maintained
rather consistently. While each subsequent photographer o
---
 Native Americans attempts to distance hersel
---
 or himsel
---
 
---
rom all previous work (possibly as a sales strategy, as well as to avoid the not uncommon charges
o
---
 photographic exploitation applied to earlier practitioners), the photography, except 
---
or various technical innovations, is remarkably the same.
There is simply limited space allotted in the West’s representation o
---
 the
Rest. This may be beginning to change with new readings (viewings) o
---

the corpus, but signiﬁcantly it is challenged most obviously in the small
corpus o
---
 Navajo photographers at work.
The two Navajo photographers illustrated here are interesting in this regard. Is there space in the chart o
---
 representations 
---
or them? I think there
is, but in alternative and interesting ways largely unavailable in the published corpus o
---
 Western photographers. They rest, at least in the readings
(viewings) I have given them, as alternatives to the registers outlined in
table 1. Tin Horn turns an intended documentary and recording photograph into an engaging snapshot, rather like a warm 
---
amily photo. But it
is clearly o
---
 the wrong 
---
amily. This was very likely done in violation o
---
 the
white man’s (and undoubtedly camera owner’s) instruction. This photograph gives voice to the unvoiced. It photographs the European American,
as probably instructed, but he is moved to the side, the owner o
---
 capital
marginalized and rendered less signiﬁcant.
Hulleah J. Tsinhnahjinnie deliberately steps outside the available registers. Her overtly political expressions combine eﬀacement and resistance,
and privilege not only a Native voice but also a Native model—hersel
---
.
Tsinhnahjinnie used her own position—indeed, hersel
---
, but also her Native identity—to mock not only a government policy but the very notion
o
---
 imposed identities. She used her ‘‘ambivalence’’ in imposed identity
terms to pose an irony—rising to ridicule. She denied Western discourse,
and any possibility that a Westerner could have even made this photograph.
98

James Faris

Thus, in the case o
---
 two selected Navajo photographers—one almost
accidental, the other subtle and pro
---
essional—we can see space that they
liberated 
---
or themselves, space unavailable in the registers argued hereto
---
ore to govern Western photographic projects o
---
 Native Americans. I am
less sanguine about the possibilities o
---
 liberating existing images o
---
 Native
Americans by Westerners or o
---
 reclaiming them to progressive ends. Perhaps they remain, however grand and aesthetically pleasing, rather like
alchemy or other great enterprises o
---
 the past. And unless one tries to
argue that medieval alchemy really did evolve and was necessary to contemporary chemistry, opening the absences or exceeding the space made
available by classic Native American photography must probably rest on
nonparadigmatic Native American photographers.

Morris Low

The Japanese Colonial Eye
SC IENC E, EXP LORATION, AND
E MP IRE

T

his essay explores the role o
---
 photography in the Japanese colonization o
---
 Manchukuo (also known as Manchoukuo or Manchuria) 
---
rom
the images in the reports o
---
 the First Scientiﬁc Expedition to Manchukuo in 1933, in which photography is prominent. The Japanese used colonial/neocolonial science as part o
---
 the process o
---
 colonization to demonstrate the need to bring modernity to the region. The term ‘‘colonial
science’’ re
---
ers to the overt use o
---
 scientiﬁc research 
---
or territorial expansion and domination, and also to the use o
---
 a colony or territory as a
source o
---
 scientiﬁc and o
---
ten commercially use
---
ul material and data by
the home country. The expedition was 
---
ormed in May with the 
---
ull team
arriving in Manchukuo in July and leaving in October. At the time Japanese photography was expanding into journalism and scientiﬁc studies,
and the in
---
ormation given in the expedition reports is designed to provide
evidence 
---
or how 
---
ar the Japanese had come compared to the less-civilized
people whom they colonized. Photography helped authorize the colonization by authenticating the conditions in Manchukuo, thereby providing
an important tool 
---
or taking stock o
---
 Japan’s in
---
ormal empire. By making
Manchukuo visible it became appropriable (Friedberg 1993, 15), but barely
had photography played its part in the appropriation be
---
ore it was being
questioned by the Japanese themselves.

Colonial Science in Manchuria
Manchuria was the subject o
---
 colonial science well be
---
ore the Japanese
arrived. In 1896 the Russian botanist Vladmir Komarov and two other naturalists used their time there not only to make botanical collections but to
explore the regions where the lines 
---
or the new Manchurian railway were
to be marked out (Bretschneider 1898; Noda 1971). By the end o
---
 the RussoJapanese War in 1905, the Japanese had won dominance over southern
Manchuria, and the rail lines 
---
rom Mukden to the port o
---
 Dairen (Dalian;

---
ormerly known as Dalny) had been built by the Russians along the line
surveyed by the naturalists.
The South Manchuria Railway Company (smr) was established by Japan
in 1906, with hal
---
 o
---
 the original capital o
---
 the company being provided
by the Japanese government. The smr dominated the Manchurian economy with its control over railways, coal mines, and industrial plants. The
railways provided Japan with the means by which to exploit Manchuria’s
natural resources, as well as the territory beyond its borders. The next 
---
ew
decades saw a period o
---
 intense railroad rivalry between China, Russia, and
Japan, in which economic and territorial rights were at stake (Wei 1980).
The ﬁrst president o
---
 the smr was Gotō Shinpei, who had been chie
---
 civil
administrator in Taiwan during the period 1898 to 1906. Gotō advocated a
scientiﬁc approach to colonialism that stressed the need 
---
or research 
---
or
colonial development and that included the use o
---
 railways as a 
---
orce 
---
or
‘‘progress’’ (Young 1966). In Gotō’s eyes, colonial policy must restructure
the social and physical environment in order 
---
or certain social changes and

The proximate excuse to do this did not, however, come

---

The Japanese news media enthusiastically promoted the war, with newspapers vying with each other to track the progress o
---

It can thus be said that the conﬂict with China was accompanied by ‘‘war

---
ever’’ (Young 1998, 58), and the news war in Japan in turn encouraged technological innovation in newspaper production and the diﬀusion o

---

important role.
The new name o

on the Manchurian space, and it was a means by which the Japanese established their domain. As in China, the Japanese imposed their own place

---
ormally a colony, the Japanese gained virtual control by
the establishment o
---

The First Scientiﬁc Expedition
As Ste

The militaristic tone o

---

the new territories under Japanese control (Iizawa 1995).
The extended photographs in the expedition report are joined together
like a panorama, allowing spatial mobility and giving the viewer some
sense o
---

and 1930s the Japanese were introduced to new ways o
---

thanks to the ‘‘real’’ photographs provided by science. Whether it be the

---

---

---

equipment to record the event. Observation o
---

---
acilitated deﬁning the Japanese national character. Protected by troops and
with progress checked via plane, the expedition was not unlike tourism.
Mark Elliott has recently explained that, even 
---
or Chinese, up until the

A mani
---

Prompted, no doubt, by such urgings, the 1930s saw a boom in Japanese

The expedition’s report helped construct Manchukuo as a tourist attraction by providing the documentation (MacCannell 1976, 41). Like all good
theme parks, there was the threat o
---

anti-Japanese guerrillas whom they invariably described as ‘‘bandits.’’ It has
been estimated that in mid-1932 there were some 300,000 to 400,000 such
people in Manchukuo opposing the Japanese army, but by the time o
---

expedition their numbers had been greatly reduced (Wilson 1995, 263).
The photographs in the expedition’s general report are numbered and

nature, and tell the narrative o
---

or consumption by the reader/potential settler (Stewart 1993, 146). As Tokunaga, the expedition leader, relates,
‘‘prior to proceeding to the research we solemnly per
---
ormed the inauguration ceremony at Hsinking on August 2, 1933, and on October 12 all o
---

---
or a time making the expected investigation and research. As to the entire course in details [sic] I have explained
with illustrations in Section I o
---

circular nature o
---

seen as a use
---
ul way to convey to readers the sense o
---

the expedition.

devoid o
---

Masculinity and Sel
---
-Cultivation
The expedition’s journey served a variety o
---

way o
---

---

---

---

---
ormed by 
---

in the presence o
---

---

the men subsequently experienced made them more conscious o
---
 their
bodies: ‘‘Situated in the southern extremity o
---

---

At the end o
---

---

---
or the Japanese. On 12 October, a dispersion ceremony was held at Hsinking Shrine, where the expedition began. The photograph o
---

(ﬁg. 6) shows members o
---

---

Imperial Palace o
---

From the images o
---

---

o

Joshua Fogel has written o
---
 the research trips o
---
 third-year students
o
---
 the Shanghai-based Tō-A Dōbun Shoin (East Asian Common Culture
Academy), which was established by the adventurer Arao Kiyoshi and
would eventually become a university. The institution stressed practical
training on contemporary aspects o
---

annual summer vacation trips were an extension o
---

---

o
---

pages) on various aspects o
---

---

documents helped Japan stake its claim in Manchukuo. And, in 
---
orming
part o
---

---

the texts reveal the various discourses at work.
In 1933, the same year as the expedition, eighty-six o
---

who participated in the annual research trip went to Manchukuo. Divided
into twenty-six groups, they scattered throughout the countryside. Like
the members o
---

---

people residing there, but perhaps because o
---

they tended to be more sympathetic to the plight o
---

and they questioned the Japanese sense o
---

185–87). The students’ involvement with Chinese society and culture was
deeper than that o
---

o
---

---

and their objects o
---

‘‘These photographs show how, through clever posing, war pictures can be

---

---
or photographic purposes ( Japan

On 7 July 1937, a clash occurred between Japanese and Chinese troops at
Marco Polo Bridge near Peking. That month, the Japanese imperial army
and navy prohibited the publication o
---

to a 
---
ull-scale conﬂict, and in September imports o
---

The Japanese Colonial Eye

117

Conclusion
As Vera Mackie (2000, 196) has argued, Japanese modernity was indeed a
colonial modernity, and colonial space could at times provide an imaginary space in which the Japanese could carve out their own dreams. The
scientiﬁc objectiﬁcation o
---
 Manchukuo in the expedition’s reports 
---
acilitated the appropriation o
---
 its history and its 
---
uture. While some o
---
 the
rhetoric was pan-Asian, it is clear that the expedition was done 
---
or the
greater glory o
---
 the emperor o
---
 Japan and the nation he symbolized.
The expedition also served to rein
---
orce the sel
---
-worth o
---
 the Japanese as
men, scientists, and colonizers. Jehol province was virgin territory and the
men could assert themselves there, sa
---
e in the knowledge that they had the
scientiﬁc tools and military might to do so. The photographs re
---
erred to
in this essay rein
---
orced the diﬀerence between the more urbane members
o
---
 the expedition to Manchukuo and the ‘‘backward’’ subjects o
---
 territories under Japanese control. The photographs reﬂect the eye o
---
 Japanese
scientists who, whether they liked it or not, became colonizing subjects.
The emptiness o
---
 Jehol rein
---
orced the notion that it was ready 
---
or colonization, and images o
---
 the ‘‘uncivilized’’ people were evidence that they
could beneﬁt 
---
rom Japanese medical science and better hygiene.
Through the use o
---
 Western science and the camera, the Japanese sought
to impose a modernity on the Chinese and the people o
---
 Manchuria as
part o
---
 their own colonialist project. We can conceive the use o
---
 photography as part o
---
 cross-cultural communication, a spectatorship that provokes thoughts and action. A certain ‘‘look’’ involves power relations that
can subjugate or empower. But the empire could strike back, and the Japanese became all too aware that the object o
---
 their gaze could manipulate
visual images as well.

118

Morris Low

Nicolas Peterson

The Changing Photographic Contract
A B ORIGIN E S A N D IMAGE ETHICS

U

ntil the late 1960s, anthropologists and others in Australia published
images o
---
 restricted Aboriginal religious activity with little thought
o
---
 possible Aboriginal concerns about such publication. Although this
practice is inde
---
ensible 
---
rom a contemporary point o
---
 view it seemed unproblematic until that time, partly because it had been going on 
---
or over
a hundred years and partly because the vast majority o
---
 images published
were in academic journals or ethnographies diﬃcult to obtain. Further, the
great social, cultural, and spatial distance between the Aboriginal people o
---

remote Australia, where the images were taken, and the regions o
---
 settled
Australia, where the images circulated, meant that the images rarely 
---
ound
their way back to the communities or areas in which they would cause
concern.
In the immediate post–World War II period what protests there were
about photography were mainly 
---
rom the government in the context o
---

the assimilation policy, and largely pertained to images that perpetuated
negative stereotypes such as those o
---
 urban or rural poverty or o
---
 people a
---

---
ected by alcohol.1 Since the late 1960s, however, the situation has changed
dramatically. Publication o
---
 images o
---
 restricted religious activity is now
unthinkable, even by popular magazines.
In this essay I want to look at the emergence o
---
 a public concern with
image ethics in relation to photography involving indigenous people and
their culture in Australia. Image ethics go 
---
ar beyond the publishing o
---


pictures o
---
 restricted activities or objects, o
---
 course, to issues o
---
 representation and in
---
ormed consent, but as 
---
ar as such a history o
---
 public concern can be established, it is the conﬂicts around this matter in particular
that have been central to the emergence o
---
 a public consciousness o
---
 the
issue. Furthermore, anthropologists, being among the principal makers,
publishers, and precipitators o
---
 conﬂict over such images, have been central to this history.
Although the ethical issues extend worldwide, it is mainly in the case
o
---
 Fourth World people, such as indigenous Australians or native North
Americans (e.g., see Brumbaugh 1996; Faris 1996; Powers 1996), where
the anthropologists, photographers, and the public archives holding the
images are within the same polity, that there is now unavoidable pressure
to address these issues. The past carelessness o
---
 anthropologists and others
in their treatment o
---
 images was brought about not just by colonial inequalities, pragmatics, and situational ethics but also by a more principled,
even i
---
 problematic, scientiﬁc concern with the importance o
---
 open inquiry and 
---
reedom o
---
 access to in
---
ormation (see Powers 1996 regarding
archives), all o
---
 which remain relevant today.
Because the vast majority i
---
 not all the photographic contracts entered
into with Aboriginal people, until recently, would have been verbal, the
amount o
---
 evidence in relation to the number o
---
 photographs taken is inﬁnitesimal. What 
---
ollows is inevitably sketchy, but it provides a preliminary outline o
---
 the history o
---
 image ethics, including the emergence o
---

public concern about the terms o
---
 the photographic contract between Aboriginal Australians and others and the consequences 
---
or the prevalence o
---

certain kinds o
---
 images. This history can be divided into three phases: 1840s
to c.1920, c.1920 to 1971, and 1971 to the present. I shall begin with two incidents, one 
---
rom 1891 and the other 
---
rom 1997, that show how remarkably
the situation has changed.

Two Photographic Encounters
On 3 September 1891 the Elder Scientiﬁc Exploring Expedition, having
reached a position near the Barrow Range in the Great Victoria Desert
in Western Australia, was suﬀering

The 1840s to the 1920s
It is a commonplace that ethnographic photography in the colonial period
encodes power relations, and that Aboriginal people were not only subjects o
---
 but also were subjected to the camera. However until the arrival
o
---
 the pocket Kodak camera in Australia in 1895, 
---
ollowing the invention
The Changing Photographic Contract

123

o
---
 roll ﬁlm in 1888, photography in the studio was much easier than it was
outdoors, and the snapshot was not yet a real possibility (see Davies and
Stanbury 1985, 1; Willis 1988, 126). Any photographic encounter required
a signiﬁcant level o
---

---
rom the Aboriginal people concerned,
except in special circumstances such as the Elder Expedition photograph
or in cases o
---

---

---

Setting up was o
---

---

the need 
---
or people to pose 
---
or the camera, even i
---

still in 
---
ront o
---

---

---

---

---

---

---

---

but, in return, they appeared always willing to render any assistance in chopping wood, &c. At length, Mr. Kilburn succeeded, and the result is here presented to the reader. (53)

Two

‘‘You’re another.’’ (1924, 133–34)

Undoubtedly Aboriginal people were under considerable pressure to be
photographed, especially at the turn o
---
 the century when photographic
technology had improved. An indication o
---
 this pressure is encapsulated
in an early-twentieth-century postcard caption: ‘‘She Views the Photo.
Fiend’’ (ﬁg. 6). The postcard shows a woman holding a large mat and a basket made by her, and she stares at the camera with a quizzical, i
---
 not hostile, look.7 The caption makes it clear that the photographer is a highly
enthusiastic devotee o
---
 photography, and 
---
urther suggests that he may not
have sought permission to take the image (although the stationary posture
130

Nicolas Peterson

and the orientation o
---
