---
alls on the 
---
ace, makes
this unlikely). I
---
 the photographer did pose the subject then the implication o
---

would bother to have taken this image. Perhaps both implications might
be picked up by viewers. The image and caption elicited an interesting response 
---
rom one viewer, a man writing to an unmarried woman: ‘‘I wish
you had been 
---
acing the photo ﬁend when stuck in the mud. . . .’’ The comment is interesting in that it shows a certain empathy with the Aboriginal
woman in the image—I wish you too had been caught in an awkward position—suggesting that the sender was responding to the look on the 
---
ace.
Overall, it can be said that photographers in this period generally had
easy access to Aboriginal subjects. For the most part the Aboriginal people
would have been shielded 
---
rom knowledge o
---

---

taken, especially when published in books with limited distribution. Most
sacred ceremonial photographs were published in such volumes, with
the major exception being the leading Sydney commercial photographer
Charles Kerry’s set o
---

---
ound their way to the
Quambone population is not known).

The 1920s to 1971
The conditions o
---
 access to Aboriginal people 
---
or pro
---
essional photographers began to change in the 1920s with the growth o
---
 the reserve system.
Aboriginal people in settled Australia were in many places removed to reserves, and because o
---
 this relocation they became less easy to photograph,
not only because the reserves were o
---
ten out o
---
 the way but also because
permission to visit them had to be sought 
---
rom the superintendents o
---

the reserves. More generally, with the demise o
---
 the social-evolutionary

---
ramework in academic circles there was much less interest in Aboriginal
people. Further, 
---
or reasons that Pinney (1992, 81–82) discusses, photographs
largely disappeared 
---
rom monographs in this period.8
While there were plenty o
---
 people who took photographs o
---
 Aboriginal people, the majority were not commercial photographers but people
engaged in anthropology, such as Donald Thomson and Charles Mount
---
ord, or those who were doing missionary work—both pro
---
essions with
privileged access to Aboriginal communities.9 Although much o
---
 this photographic work circulated through academic journals and monographs,
The Changing Photographic Contract

131

some o
---

---
or example,
Thomson in the Illustrated London News, and both Thomson and Mount
---
ord
in National Geographic) or in popular books.10
The assimilation policy acted as a 
---
urther restraint on commercial photography in the immediate postwar period, as bureaucrats were o
---
ten suspicious o
---
 photographers documenting conditions that were an embarrassment to the government or mission body administering the village on
the government’s behal
---

---

Western Australia (
---
or example, see Grayden 1957, 22). The 
---

---

‘‘be
---

A. Poignant 1957 and R. Poignant 1996).11
These trends aﬀected publicly available images quite dramatically, as the

---

The physical and cultural gul
---

protected anthropologists and others 
---
rom 
---

---

---
or example, Simpson 1951),14 and they continued

---

---

largely irrelevant to people who could not read, and the 
---
ew that did ﬁnd

---

---
ew restricted images 
---
ound their way onto postcards because commercial photographers rarely had time to build up the links that would allow them to
attend the restricted ceremonies.16
Films were the ﬁrst images to reach remote Aboriginal communities
and create major concern among the people there. Almost universally in
remote Aboriginal communities during the 1960s and 1970s there was a
Friday or Saturday evening ﬁlm show attended by almost everybody. Film
programs were put together by the national ﬁlm lending library in Canberra and normally included a short ﬁlm (o
---
ten a documentary) along with
132

Nicolas Peterson

a popular 
---
eature ﬁlm, and these programs then circulated 
---
rom community to community. The two documentary ﬁlms that caused the ﬁrst major
consternation in the early 1960s were made by David Attenborough—Artists o
---
 Arnhem Land and Desert Gods—both o
---
 which included restricted ritual
sequences (see Attenborough 1963). Presumably the ﬁlms were included in
the programs 
---
or Aboriginal communities because the people in Canberra
thought they would be o
---
 local interest.
In 1964 the Australian Institute o
---
 Aboriginal Studies, as it was then
called, embarked on a major ethnographic ﬁlmmaking program o
---
 recording men’s restricted rituals in the belie
---
 that the rituals were rapidly disappearing. By 1965 the Institute had its own ﬁlm unit virtually dedicated
to this purpose, which during the next 
---
our years produced over a dozen
ﬁlms o
---
 men’s restricted ceremonies. By 1967 questions began to emerge
about the distribution and circulation o
---
 these ﬁlms, but still at this stage
it was agreed with the participants in the ﬁlms that as long as they were
not shown in remote Australia they could be shown to anthropological
audiences in the south (see Bryson 2002).17
The Institute’s ﬁlm director’s views on the circulation o
---

dedicated to the values o
---

---
 these
extremely interesting and important ethnographic documents the Institute was elevating the narrow ethic o
---
 tribal law above the larger values
to which the Institute was, by deﬁnition, committed (see ﬁg. 7) (Sandall
1976).18
However, in 1969 the Institute began to change to a much more restrictive policy on the use and distribution o
---
 ceremonial ﬁlms made by its ﬁlm
unit, and it eventually adopted a buy-back policy in the 1970s. The audience
issues raised by this ﬁlming program and the movement to restrict their
distribution and circulation preﬁgured many o
---
 the changes that were to
take place regarding still photographs.
The transition taking place at this time is clearly captured in a paper
published by Catherine Ellis (1970) on the role o
---

the study o
---

---

image a copyright symbol and the sentence, ‘‘The taking o
---

o
---

---
orbidden.’’ This clearly recognizes that even
the obscure and specialist nature o
---
 the journal in which the paper was
published, Miscellanea Musicologica, might no longer keep the images circulating in the academic domain. Nevertheless the advancement o
---
 knowlThe Changing Photographic Contract

1971 to the Present
Although Aboriginal people achieved the 
---
ull political rights o
---

the kinds o
---

---
or this
remained the remote location o

o

---
ew Aboriginal people

Nicolas Peterson

mote Australia visited the southern part o

The 1970s saw two deﬁning events in this respect. The most crucial, because it marked an absolute watershed in anthropological use o
---

---

about the Aboriginal people o
---

---

---
ound
its way into a number o
---

---

---
rom Warburton.19 It appears
that around 15 May 1971, an Aboriginal schoolgirl 
---
rom the town o
---

---
or Warburton) who had been on a school trip
to Perth returned home with a copy o
---

attention because the woman on the cover was a close relative. She had
shown the book to her 
---
ather who became very angry because eleven o
---

the ﬁ
---
ty-two photographs showed restricted ceremonial objects and activities. Moreover, it was rumored that there was a threat to the young girl’s
li
---
e because she had seen these images.20
The subsequent events and the long-term ramiﬁcations o
---

were complex, but the immediate 
---

---
or researchers who wanted to work in Western Australian communities. A change was also made to the research permit granting
system so that instead o

---

---
are, the Aboriginal community concerned also would be involved. This move galvanized the anthropological
world, and the Australian Institute o

---

Access,’’ to be held 9–10 August 1971, at which the

Publisher’s note
Readers are asked to cooperate with a speciﬁc condition placed on secret/
sacred material in this book by Aboriginal men and/or women. All knowledge relating to these rituals (sites) is normally conﬁned to the men and/or
women who have been inducted into them. Many Aborigines are eager to

ore it has been established that he has the correct standing in

This was the Institute on the cusp o
---

The second deﬁning event secured a more or less complete transition to
a virtual ban on all publication o
---

---

Pitjantjatjara Council sought an interlocutory injunction against both anthropologist Charles Mount

---

Nicolas Peterson

Nomads o

This led to the book being withdrawn

rom publication (see also Pitjantjatjara Council and Peter Nganingu v. John Lowe and Lyn Bender, in McCorquodale

Concomitant with these changes was the beginning o
---

opposite direction. As Aboriginal people became more

own photographs, the widespread aversion to seeing pictures o

similar in

many areas pictures o

---

---

Conclusion
Since the mid-1970s Aboriginal people have gained a great degree o
---
 control over the reproduction o
---
 photographic images that involve them. The
highwater mark o
---
 this control was the Australian Institute o
---
 Aboriginal
and Torres Strait Islander Studies’ bicentennial volume, A
---
ter Two Hundred
Years: Photographic Essays o
---
 Aboriginal and Islander Australia today (Taylor 1988),
in which Aboriginal communities both negotiated the terms on which the
photographers involved in the project entered their communities and participated in selecting and captioning the images in the volume. The Institute also put out a primer 
---
or the photographers involved in the project
concerning restrictions on taking photographs in Aboriginal communities
(Michaels 1994). Although it was the events outlined above that brought
The Changing Photographic Contract

137

the ethical issues o
---
 the photographic contract to the 
---
ore and changed
the practice o
---
 anthropologists and others, it is land rights, as the Institute
con
---
erence 
---
oresaw, that has given Aboriginal people real control.
A case in point is the immediate consequence o
---
 the handover to the
Pitjantjatjara in October 1985 o
---
 Uluru (Ayers Rock), one o
---
 the major tourist sites in the country, thereby giving Aboriginal people a majority on the
management board o
---
 this national park. Among the changes was the erection o
---
 the 
---
ollowing notice:
photographs
Traditional owners request that you respect their customs & re
---
rain 
---
rom
photographing local Aboriginal people.
Having one’s photograph taken is considered culturally inappropriate as the
image captured on ﬁlm, believed to be part o
---
 one’s spirit, is removed 
---
rom
that persons control 
---
orever.
Thank you 
---
or your co-operation. (photo in Weekend Australian Magazine, 18–
19 October 1986, 3)

Ironically the photograph adjacent to the image o
---
 the notice in the Weekend Australian was o
---
 a Pitjantjatjara man, Tony Tjamiwa, who was interviewed 
---
or the article and apparently allowed his photograph to be taken.
This inconsistency suggests that the writer o
---
 the notice had some di
---
ﬁculty articulating the grounds on which Aboriginal people objected to
having their photograph taken. Although 
---
ear o
---
 being photographed has,
as indicated above, been present since photography began in Australia, and
there are a number o
---
 cases reported where it was seen as soul the
---
t or the
like, there are good grounds now 
---
or being skeptical o
---
 this reason 
---
or the
objection to having a photograph taken. Apart 
---
rom anything else Aboriginal people o
---
 the area have had their photographs taken 
---
or many years
and, in the past, numbers o
---
 them have negotiated 
---
or payment with tourists who wanted to take their photograph. Many Aboriginal people now
have cameras, and old and new photographs are much valued.
The diﬃculties around Uluru are that it is sold as a tourist destination in
which Aboriginal culture 
---
eatures strongly and it is an attraction to tourists. Understandably, tourists are keen to have photographs o
---
 Aboriginal
people in keeping with the overall climate o
---
 commercialization o
---
 their
culture, and they tend to take photographs o
---
 Aboriginal people unasked.
In part this may be due to diﬀerent understandings o
---
 what is public and
what is private space. Although Uluru is deﬁnitely public space 
---
rom a non-

138

Nicolas Peterson

Aboriginal point o
---
 view, 
---
rom an Aboriginal point o
---
 view it is clearly
seen as an Aboriginal space in which Europeans are outsiders. As such it is
not hard to imagine that Aboriginal people 
---
eel that the behavior o
---
 nonAboriginal people should be circumspect and that permission should be
asked by people wishing to take their photograph.
There are situations that 
---
acilitate photographing people with whom one
does not have a personal relationship. Principal among these is being outdoors in a public place where a 
---
ormal or in
---
ormal per
---
ormance, activity,

---
estive occasion, or demonstration is going on. Thus i
---
 Aboriginal people
are dancing or demonstrating some technique or aspect o
---
 their material
culture at a tourist destination or public gathering, photographing them
unasked is usually unproblematic, and even photographing those people
observing the per
---
ormance is usually achieved without diﬃculty because
observation and photography are components o
---
 the situation—indeed,
an audience is usually required. As Henderson (1988, 97–98) says in her discussion o
---
 access and consent in photography in public places, as a person’s
activity becomes less 
---
ocused and more personal so it becomes more private even in a public space, and the pressure to secure consent becomes
stronger. People generally do not like being singled out, especially when
there is no clear reason why.
Photographing children is an exception to these constraints (ﬁg. 8). Children are less 
---
ully cultural or social than adults, and as such are seen and
treated as moral juniors. Parents and other adults can give consent to them
being photographed, even against their will. Commonly, however, they are
willing collaborators with the camera, and it is a great deal easier to get
spontaneous images o
---
 them because, depending on age, they are usually
less sel
---
-conscious about their appearance and, as Henderson says, ‘‘less
likely to anticipate the ‘possible horrors’ o
---
 photographs as they might appear in publication’’ (1988, 98). Further, such images can o
---
ten have a universal appeal. Noel Wallace, who with his wi
---
e, Phyl, published a photographic book on Pitjantjatjara children, has this to say:
Photographing the Pitjantjatjara children is a pleasant task, but not without
its own peculiar ‘‘occupational hazards.’’ The more sophisticated children,
who know about cameras, line up and display toothy smiles as soon as they
see one appear; the less sophisticated, with the natural curiosity o
---
 children
everywhere, want to see what you are doing—with the result that the scene
in the viewing screen disappears, and a single, very out-o
---
-
---
ocus eye takes
its place—or you ﬁnd three or

TH E PA PUA N EW GU IN E A PHOTOGRAPHS

1899 – 1907

T

he photographic collection housed in the basement o
---
 the Royal Anthropological Institute (rai) in London contains more than 1,500 glass
negatives taken in Papua New Guinea by Captain Francis R. Barton between 1899 and 1907. During this period Barton was government secretary,
resident magistrate, and ﬁnally administrator o
---
 the Territory. He was a
keen and proliﬁc photographer and took hundreds o
---
 photographs around
Port Moresby and on his oﬃcial tours; images through which we can trace
his encounters with diverse Papuan cultures. But he also took a considerable number o
---
 overtly staged photographs o
---
 young girls, and it is one
o
---
 these that I want to consider here. I have chosen to 
---
ocus on this one
image, with all its attendant ‘‘diﬃculties,’’ in order to raise a series o
---
 questions about contemporary critical approaches to such early photographs.
The presence o
---
 the photographs as material arti
---
acts housed in the rai archive allows me to per
---
orm this work on them, and it is their history as
arti
---
acts that also concerns me here.
The photograph o
---
 a Hula girl called Luikama 
---
rom the southeast coast
o
---
 Papua New Guinea (ﬁg. 1) was taken by Barton in Port Moresby probably sometime between 1904 and 1907, the period during which he developed this particular style o
---
 his photographic work. The photograph

1. A Hula girl
called Luikama,
Port Moresby. Photo
by Francis R. Barton,
probably c.1904–1907.
Courtesy o
---
 the Royal
Anthropological
Institute (rai
no. 20524).

presents a challenge, the hand-drawn box in the center o
---
 the photo con
---
ronts us directly with Barton’s intention(s), and also makes us acutely
aware o
---
 our own intentions in looking at the photograph. The box has a
disturbing tactile quality, and the acts o
---

---
raming that it signiﬁes can be unsettling, at least to an audience that wants to ‘‘understand’’
the photograph within an anthropological 
---

---

---
erred to, are vitally important here. The
photograph challenges us to create a narrative 
---
or it; it suggests that there
is a beyond to which it can lead us. But how, and within whose history,
can this photograph be inscribed and assimilated, and what is at stake in
this process? Through an extended consideration o
---
 this one photograph
(although bringing in several others as counterpoints) I want to trace some
o
---

The photograph’s sexual content, and intent, is seemingly obvious. Luikama’s pose, the decontextualization o
---
 her body, her display and circulation as an object—both within the photograph and as a photograph—are
all the products o
---
 a colonial desire.1 It is as though Barton were merely
Supple Bodies

147

per
---
orming or reenacting a colonial gaze o
---
 which Luikama is the victim.
The unequal power relation on which the photograph rests—Barton the
colonial oﬃcial, Luikama the ‘‘innocent native’’—allows the projection o
---

a stereotype, and the photograph becomes an example o
---
 colonial and
anthropological surveillance in which Luikama is reduced to an object. In
this respect it is perhaps the complicity or contamination o
---

with Barton’s that is potentially unsettling. However, I am wary o
---

such understandings o
---

---

---

recognize both some o
---

---
 the encounter
and some o
---

o
---
 others.
The photograph was taken, to consider just one aspect o
---
 intention, in
the name o
---
 an ethnographic science. Barton’s interest was, at some nominal level at least, in Luikama’s tattoos. This is why he drew a 
---
rame on
the glass negative, isolating her tattooed armpit. Yet, surely this is not the
subject o
---
 the photograph? Tattoos were 
---
requently represented in lateeighteenth and early-nineteenth-century engravings as disembodied body
parts, an array o
---

---
aces (Thomas 1997, 68), and
this 
---
ragmentation and objecti
---
ying style was continued in later photographic representations o
---
 tattoos. But here, the hand-drawn 
---
rame within
a 
---
rame is ambiguous; in isolating and producing the ‘‘scientiﬁc detail’’ it
simultaneously 
---
etishizes Luikama’s body and reveals Barton’s intervention. It makes Barton’s gaze all the more obvious, and in so doing paradoxically threatens the very aura o
---
 science that provided the impetus 
---
or
its addition. Here ethnography is a pretext, a ruse that allowed Barton to
indulge his voyeurism, the re
---
erencing o
---
 tattooing serving to veil what
might otherwise make the photograph less acceptable within the context
o
---
 ethnographic science. The photograph o
---

overlapping series o
---

---
ying that were central to processes o
---
 colonial appropriation.
This, then, is an initial inscription: Barton’s attempt to redeem the photograph 
---
or ethnographic science, as though by drawing the box as an a
---
terthought he could somehow trans
---
orm the glass plate in some alchemical
way. Inscriptions like this are per
---
ormances through which photographs
acquire meanings and are embedded in histories. A series o
---

which 
---
unction as 
---
rames or contexts, can be traced through the photograph; 
---
rom ethnographic science to classical or romantic idealism, and
pornography. Hundreds o
---

---

similar to this one o
---

Christopher Wright

drawn box) were oﬀered 
---
or sale as prints in the busy carte de visite and

---

to move 
---
rom one site o
---

Barton considered himsel
---

---

o

him when Seligman visited Papua New Guinea in 1904 as part o

Daniels Expedition. Barton carried out some ﬁeld research 
---

and the two kept up a written correspondence. In 1910 Barton contributed

---

At some level Barton saw himsel
---

---

Barton also drew lines on bodies as well as on glass negatives (ﬁg. 2). 2
Tattoos presented certain technical diﬃculties 
---
or early photographers in
the Paciﬁc, and their lack o
---

commented on. Barton solved this problem by painting over existing tattoos, tracing their exact shapes in his own mixture o
---

---
or photographing. However, Seligman

---

---

---

---

---

external intervention in order to make things 
---
ully visible. Yet his process
o
---

---
using here; Barton painstakingly painted over
Luikama’s tattoos to make them stand out and thus allow their 
---
ull observation, but he then posed her and composed the photograph in a highly
stylized manner, be
---
ore ﬁnally, a
---
ter the glass negative had been developed,
drawing the box to isolate the ‘‘scientiﬁc detail.’’ It is as i
---
 the ‘‘
---
ocus’’ o
---

photograph were not considered suﬃcient on its own.
Thus the photograph becomes a record o
---

---
ormance o
---

---

---

through which Luikama’s body is territorialized. However, this reading is
to some extent based on a series o
---

colonialism through which certain inscriptions are linked to

orm trajecSupple Bodies

One reading o
---
 Luikama’s tattoos by a Victorian audience, both popular
and scientiﬁc, would have been as indicators o
---
 an assumed ‘‘savagery.’’ Tat150

Christopher Wright

toos marked physical and cultural diﬀerence, and, like other sur
---
ace eﬀects
such as physical de
---
ormities, were 
---
requently understood by some latenineteenth-century European audiences in terms o
---
 their links to a depth,
a ‘‘primitive’’ interior that is also revealed in the photograph. Cesar Lombroso, one o
---
 the 
---
ounders o
---
 modern criminology, argued in 1911 that the
desire to be tattooed was a biological 
---
eature o
---
 the criminal ‘‘type,’’ a kind
o
---
 genetic disposition that o
---
ten came along with a passion 
---
or other 
---
orms
o
---
 excessive ornamentation, bodily mutilation, strange clothing, and obscure slang (Lombroso, cited in Gell 1993, 12). These dispositions were also
considered to be shared by ‘‘primitive humanity’’—not in the sense that
they were similarly regarded as criminals, but that both were considered to
be lower on an evolutionary scale than ‘‘civilized’’ society, and thus somehow morally deﬁcient or degraded. Yet we must also remain aware that
there were other audiences, such as sailors, soldiers, or those considered
part o
---
 the Victorian ‘‘underworld,’’ to whom tattoos—although not necessarily those o
---
 someone like Luikama—indicated a sense o
---
 belonging and
a positive diﬀerence, among a range o
---
 other connotations. What is important here is the 
---
ormation o
---
 a group or nexus o
---
 related inscriptions—
the links that connect various Victorian notions o
---
 ‘‘savage,’’ ‘‘criminal,’’
morally degenerate, and sexually illicit—that cluster around Luikama’s tattoos and 
---
orm one trajectory 
---
or the photograph.
Facial and bodily blemishes were 
---
requently edited out o
---
 European
portrait photographs o
---
 the late nineteenth century precisely because it
was thought that they too readily revealed inner deﬁciencies o
---
 the sel
---
.
Through a similar play between sur
---
ace and depth, nineteenth-century
audiences, and indeed contemporary ones, might see Luikama’s individuality revealed in the photograph. Although these might be widely divergent notions o
---
 individuality, we still tend to make the equation between
a photographic portrait and an individual as though they were in some respects equal units. We press the shutter release and capture the individual,
and in achieving this act there is a sense in which photography retains some
o

Christopher Wright

created a strong association between children’s bodies and a sense o

Through the Victorian cult o

a per

---
 the undecorated body—exempliﬁed by Greek sculpture—
as literally embodying the height o
---
 civilization; and Hula ideas o
---
 body
decoration as precisely that which deﬁnes human qualities. How Victorian and Edwardian notions o
---
 ‘‘natural innocence,’’ particularly in relation
to photographs o
---
 children, might contrast or overlap with Hula notions
o
---
 nature and culture is another history o
---
 the photograph that could be
traced across cultural borders.
Rather than 
---
ocusing solely on a colonial gaze and the construction o
---

various stereotypes, it is necessary to track how these interact with indigenous constructions. The photograph o
---
 Luikama is entangled in the histories o
---
 diﬀerent scopic strategies, not just that o
---
 colonial surveillance and
its object.
The androgynous status o
---
 children in some Melanesian cultures is a

---
eature 
---
requently commented on by anthropologists. In order to assume
a place in the reproductive order the androgynous child must be actively
placed in a single-sex category, and the body is 
---
requently the site o
---
 this
construction. Marilyn Strathern has discussed how Melanesian initiation
ceremonies, rather than making a sel
---
-contained individual, o
---
ten serve
to make individuals incomplete: ‘‘What is produced is a sexually activated
person, a potential reproducer, an incomplete person whose identity must
be completed in relation with another’’ (1988, 27). In many Melanesian cultures a person’s identity is the product o
---
 a complex set o
---
 social relations,
the links she or he has with other individuals rather than the possession o
---

an individual.
In contrast to Victorian culture, the body in Hula culture is not seen
as the expression o
---
 an inner individuality, the sign o
---
 a person belonging
Supple Bodies

163

to her or himsel
---
. So, tattoos, rather than the sur
---
ace expression o
---
 an interior individuality are, 
---
or the Hula, evidence o
---
 an exterior individuality
that is constructed through relations with others. For the Hula it is not the
individual that is tattooed, but rather a speciﬁc kind o
---
 individual that is
partially created through the external relations that are realized in both
making and displaying the tattoos. Tattoos are the evidence, the mani
---
estations o
---
 these external relations (Strathern 1997, 99).
Be
---
ore tattoos make sense to any audience or reader, they give pain to
the living body, they hurt. The Hula eye that looks at tattoos does not just
read them, it also winces, it senses the pain. The Hula body is malleable,
a ground 
---
or inscription. Barton treated tattoos as the inscription o
---
 an
evolutionary narrative, he wanted to discover a vocabulary o
---
 tattooing by
tracing the designs o
---
 tattoos back to their roots in Melanesian and Southeast Asian words 
---
or various animals (1918). For Barton the role o
---
 Hula
tattoos is to signi
---
y, to eﬀace themselves be
---
ore a meaning that is located
elsewhere, in a complex table o
---
 orthographic similarities or in an assumed
‘‘primitiveness’’ or ‘‘innocence.’’ For the Hula tattoos involve beauty and
sociality, they are 
---
orces as much as signs.
Lutkehaus and Roscoe (1995, 21) discuss the links between beauty and
power in Melanesia, and the way that physical attractiveness, such as that
achieved through tattooing, implies the power and the ability to seduce
both humans and valuable objects. Yet, o
---
 course, it is within the context
o
---
 Hula culture that Luikama’s display o
---
 her body has that power and eﬃcacy. Her depiction in a photograph that takes no account o
---
 that context
in its strategy o
---
 representation—that is also taken away 
---
rom local audiences and displayed to others, reproduced, circulated, and ﬁnally placed
in an archive—discounts or neutralizes that power.
Barton’s per
---
ormance in taking the photographs, and what subsequently
happens to them, ignores the social relations through which Hula sexuality
and individuality are mani
---
ested and sustained; Barton substitutes a diﬀerent set o
---
 relations and other audiences. His photographs could be seen as
an attempt to impose one type o
---
 inscription over another; to replace one
context o
---
 revelation with another.
In this light the photograph that we started with is not, then, a portrait
o
---
 Luikama—or rather it can only be seen as such within certain trajectories and in relation to particular audiences. To read the photograph as a
representation that stands 
---
or an individual is to ignore the ways in which
individuality is constructed in Hula culture. Any assumptions about the
presence o
---
 an individuality in the photograph need to be put in a cross164

Christopher Wright

cultural perspective: 
---
or many Melanesian cultures’ sel
---
-objectiﬁcation is
not a desirable outcome o
---
 any representation but a positive danger. Melanesians do not want to be seen individually, because 
---
or them individuality
lies in relationships. In an extremely thought-provoking consideration o
---

one o
---
 her own photographs o
---
 a Hagen dancer 
---
rom the highlands o
---

Papua New Guinea, Strathern discusses how the dancers’ decorations show
a capacity to draw in relationships, but are ‘‘not a portrait i
---
 we mean that
inner individuality must show in the 
---
eatures, 
---
or individuality lies not so
much in the appearance as in the act o
---
 assembling’’ (1997, 99). To say that
they are a representation implies a medium, and although we might see
the 
---
eatures o
---
 the body as a medium through which a portrait represents
the individuality o
---
 a person, this is not necessarily the case in Melanesia.
Similarly, Hula tattoos do not ‘‘represent’’ relations with others but are
only there because o
---
 them, they are the result o
---
 activated relations: as
Strathern says, ‘‘we witness an outcome: the result or eﬀect o
---
 mobilizing
relations’’ (99).
Barton’s photograph is like an attempt to tattoo Luikama, to inscribe
and ﬁx her as permanently as the pigments used to mark her skin. Both
photographs and Hula tattoos have similar eﬀects to the ‘‘initiation objects’’ o
---
 the Rauto o
---
 New Britain described by Thomas Maschio, which
‘‘can be said to memorialize the identities o
---
 past owners, when the initiates have them placed on their persons their own identities participate
in, and are augmented by, ancestral identities’’ (1995, 156). Photographs, like
tattoos, bring into being other selves, including spirit selves.7 There are
cross-cultural ideas o
---
 ‘‘containment’’ at stake here, and in some senses we
treat photographs in a way similar to Hula attitudes toward tattoos: as evidence or containers o
---
 relations. Photographs are material ‘‘arti
---
acts’’ that
demonstrate relations with others, the living and the dead, helping to 
---
ashion particular types o
---
 individuals through these relationships.
It is perhaps ﬁnally our myth o
---
 photography’s ability to steal souls, our
belie
---
 in its ability to capture individuality, that is the starting point 
---
or a
reading o
---
 Barton’s photograph o
---
 Luikama as only concerned with the imposition o
---
 a colonial gaze over an individual. This trajectory tends to pose
the problem in terms o
---
 a binary opposition between type and individual,
or object and subject, automatically privileging the latter over the 
---
ormer.
We assume the presence o
---
 an individuality in the photograph that then
contributes to a reading o
---
 it as the inscription o
---
 a stereotype that masks
that individual. But the ways in which the Hula construct individuality in
the relationships between individual and group disrupts this redemptive
Supple Bodies

165

strategy. Luikama’s individuality evades the photograph, it is located elsewhere, and we are le
---
t with the question o
---
 what a photographic representation o
---
 Hula individuality would look like. How can we, or should we,
see the individual and not the group? A consideration o
---
 Barton’s photograph in these terms is one route toward a cross-cultural understanding
o
---
 processes o
---
 representation, an understanding that would enable 
---
uture
representations o
---
 cultural Others to both take account o
---
 and convey indigenous aesthetics.8
I want neither to redeem Barton’s photograph nor to reduce Luikama to
simply the object o
---
 a colonial gaze, a project that actually avoids some o
---

the more disturbing aspects o
---
 us looking at the photographs now. We can
gesture to another point in time in which the objectiﬁcation and voyeurism took place, rather than con
---
ront the possibilities o
---
 our own. Even i
---

Barton’s photograph o
---
 Luikama did not capture her individuality in the
sense that I have discussed here, it still had, and potentially has, many adverse eﬀects that do need to be considered. Photographs involve acts as
well as representations. What I want to suggest here are other creative and
productive ways o
---
 looking at these kinds o
---
 photographs. Although understanding them as evidence o
---
 the misuse o
---
 colonial power is an important
aspect o
---
 any consideration o
---
 them, the individuality that 
---
requently gets
assumed in these histories needs to be approached more critically. In many
discussions o
---
 photographs like Barton’s the emphasis has been too heavily
weighted toward one side o
---
 the equation, on the colonial gaze itsel
---
 rather
than on the entanglement o
---
 that gaze with indigenous notions. Such a

---
ocus can retain a colonial perspective and ignores some o
---
 the borrowings
and appropriations that have occurred in both directions as a result o
---
 encounters between cultures. The pierced, scarred, and tattooed European
and American bodies displayed in recent publications like Modern Primitives (Vale and Juno 1991) show the continuing impact o
---
 perceived ‘‘tribal’’
attitudes on our own body culture.
Photographs trace multiple trajectories: 
---
or all their superﬁcial ﬁxity and
their inclusion in structures like the archive that seek to contain them,
they are processual and constantly in motion. What brings meanings to
photographs are per
---
ormances o
---
 them, speciﬁc readings and enactments.
The photograph crystalizes these per
---
ormances, providing a site where
meanings both appear and disappear. What is important is not so much
what the image contains, a meaning that resides within it, but what is
brought to it, how it is used, and how it is connected to various trajectories.
Potentially productive 
---
uture uses o
---
 collections like Barton’s include re166

Christopher Wright

patriation9 and the kind o
---
 visual work exempliﬁed by the Australian photographer Leah King-Smith. The history o
---
 photography as a technology o
---

colonial surveillance has been discussed to the point o
---
 exhaustion, and
along with the tracking o
---
 Barton’s photographs across cultural borders
these uses represent creative new directions 
---
or the history o
---
 his work as
both arti
---
acts and representations. They would also take the photographs
out o
---
 the realm o
---
 academic debate and actively connect them to other
histories and trajectories.
The supple bodies o
---
 my title are not those re
---
erred to by Barton in
his letters or articles (1908, 259) or those imagined and represented in his
photographs, but rather those o
---
 the living malleable bodies o
---
 Hula culture, a suppleness that enables their beauty and eﬃcacy. They are also the
‘‘bodies’’ o
---
 photographs and their spaces, bodies that, like those that are
tattooed, reveal our construction through relations with others.

---

S

ometime in the early 1900s, the Peruvian artist Juan Manuel Figueroa
Aznar arranged his easel, paints, stool, palette, two open photo magazines, and two portrait canvases in 
---
ront o
---
 a studio backdrop. On the backdrop, which he also used 
---
or occasional work as a commercial photographer, he had painted on one side a Spanish colonial archway and, on the
other, one hal
---
 o
---
 a traditional Andean religious altar. Having arranged his
utensils and easel in 
---
ront o
---
 this scene, Figueroa then set up his camera,
prepared the plate, composed the image, and arranged 
---
or another person
to release the shutter. He straightened his suit, adjusted the ﬂower on his
lapel, and posed, cigarette in hand and one leg crossed casually over the
other, to contemplate his work o
---
 art (ﬁg. 1).
But where, exactly, do we situate the object o
---
 this care
---
ully 
---
ramed and
contemplative gaze? Is it the not-yet-ﬁnished painting? The thematic space
uniting artist, easel, and paints? Or the still-broader 
---
rame o
---
 an anticipated
image that has just been composed on a chemically coated, and industrially produced, plate o
---
 glass? How, in short, are we to understand the relationship o
---
 this turn-o
---
-the century Peruvian dandy, photographer, and
painter to the representational technologies he so skill
---
ully wields?
A glance at the embedded pictorial 
---
rames o
---
 this particular sel
---
-portrait

suggests that Figueroa was himsel
---
 suggesting such questions about the
relationship between photography, painting, and art. The stretched and
painted canvas is 
---
ramed by an easel. The easel is in turn 
---
ramed by a
stretched and painted backdrop. This entire scene is then 
---
ramed by the
awkwardly visible and slightly rumpled upper edge o
---
 the canvas backdrop.
This interior 
---
rame—which could easily have been edited 
---
rom the plate
—dismantles the illusion a backdrop is intended to convey. Juxtaposed
next to the even sharper line le
---
t by the negative edge itsel
---
, the void le
---
t
visible by this painted backdrop’s edge reveals the photographer’s awareness o
---
 the technological and artistic artiﬁces enabling his own romanticized and introspective gaze into the world o
---
 easel, palette, and disguise.
In what 
---
ollows I suggest that Figueroa did indeed have a very particular
interest in understanding and, to a certain extent, dismantling the artiﬁces
o
---
 both photography and art. Like the dandiﬁed sel
---
-image he assumed, his
approaches to these problems borrowed heavily 
---
rom the literature and art
o
---
 European romanticism. Yet, contrary to the concept o
---
 an amusing colonial mimicry that our own ﬁrst viewing o
---
 this provincial Peruvian dandy
might evoke, his reappropriations and reworkings o
---
 these borrowed European elements were neither innocent nor misconstrued. Rather, Figueroa
created an approach to both photography and modernity that intentionally departed 
---
rom the dominant mold o
---
 European modernism. In the

---
ollowing discussion o
---
 his li
---
e, I am particularly interested in exploring
the ways in which this tangentially modernist style was shaped by Peruvian
understandings o
---

regionalist movement known as indigenismo.1

Photography and Art in Peru
Juan Manuel Figueroa Aznar was born in 1878 in the small town o
---
 Caras in
the Andean highlands o
---
 the Peruvian department o
---
 Ancash. Soon a
---
ter
Juan Manuel’s birth the 
---
amily set up residence in Lima, and it was in this
turbulent and war-torn city o
---
 the 1880s that the young Juan Manuel spent
his youth and completed his primary and high school education at the Colegio N.S. de Guadalupe.2 He then went on to study at the Academia Concha, a privately endowed municipal ﬁne arts institution in Lima. Following
his studies at the Academia, Figueroa worked his way through Ecuador,
Colombia, and Panama as a portrait painter. While in Colombia, Figueroa
may well have learned o
---
 such developments in art photography as aca174

Deborah Poole

demicism and naturalism, both o
---
 which were practiced quite early in
Colombia by Antioqueño photographers such as Melitón Rodriguez (see
Serrano 1983; Arango 1988).
The Peru to which Juan Manuel returned sometime in 1899 or 1900, however, was strikingly diﬀerent in many respects 
---
rom both Colombia and
the western European countries where art photographies ﬁrst emerged.
The vast majority o
---
 the country’s population consisted o
---
 Quechua- and
Aymara-speaking peasants employed in small-scale community agriculture or as peons on semi
---
eudal agrarian estates. The small elite o
---
 rural
landlords who owned these estates lived in provincial cities that, with

---
ew exceptions, were isolated 
---
rom the modernizing currents o
---
 industrial capitalist development.3 Lima, the capital o
---
 this predominantly rural
country, was home to a small but prosperous oligarchy whose wealth was
based on sugar and cotton plantations built 
---
rom the accumulated proﬁts
o
---
 the prewar guano (bird manure) and nitrate trade. Production in both
the guano ﬁelds and the plantations they engendered was based on indentured contract labor 
---
rom China and on sharecropping by coastal and
highland peasantries. Little or none o
---
 the income 
---
rom these properties
was invested in industrial development. As a consequence, Lima lacked the
bourgeois and emerging middle classes who served as both the European
and Colombian audiences 
---
or pictorialist and amateur photographies.4
In these diﬀerent social and class settings, ‘‘art photography’’ had
emerged to 
---
ulﬁll a historically speciﬁc set o
---
 representational tasks. These
tasks were related to the split between an aesthetic discourse based on
notions o
---
 artistic creativity, manual production, and the author; and
the 
---
orms o
---
 scientiﬁc epistemology, mechanized technology, and commodity production in
---
orming bourgeois industrial society (see Benjamin
1968; Sekula 1983, 1984). Pictorialist photographies resolved this problem
by trans
---
orming the scientiﬁc (or industrial) qualities o
---
 photography into
‘‘art’’ through the photographer-artist’s personal (i.e., manual) intervention
into the mechanical technology o
---
 
---
ocus, tone, 
---
raming, angle, and texture.
The photographic print was in this respect no longer a mass-reproducible
commodity but a unique ‘‘work o
---
 art.’’
In early-twentieth-century Lima, by comparison, the ruling class was
still ﬁrmly entrenched in its traditional modes o
---
 domination and had not
yet been con
---
ronted by the challenge o
---
 industrial 
---
orms o
---
 commodity
production or mass-marketed art (kitsch). In this society o
---
 patronage and

---
amily, success as an artist depended on access both to 
---
amily resources and
to the prestigious allure that artistic training in Europe, particularly Paris,
Figueroa Aznar and the Cusco Indigenistas

175

acquired 
---
or those artists with the means to study abroad (see Eléspuru
1968; Lauer 1976). More speciﬁcally, with respect to Figueroa’s personal
situation, success as a visual artist meant success as a painter, and painters
had not yet been 
---
orced to con
---
ront the artistic marketplace whose traumas had paved the way 
---
or both pictorial modernism and art photography
in Europe.
The 
---
ailure o
---
 aestheticized and pictorialist photographies to take hold
in Lima must, then, be understood with respect to the speciﬁc class and
social structures that deﬁned contemporary Peruvian reality. This 
---
act has
two important consequences 
---
or how we read Figueroa’s photographs.
First, it suggests that Figueroa’s photographic work was never considered,
or perhaps intended, to be ‘‘art.’’ Second, it suggests that Figueroa’s early decision to turn to photography was not so much an artistic choice as a practical solution to the dilemma created by his relatively marginal social position in the closed and highly stratiﬁed society o
---
 oligarchic Lima. For an
artist with neither upper-class 
---
amily ties nor European training, apprenticeship in a success
---
ul photography studio was one o
---
 the 
---
ew available
routes through which to pursue a viable career in portraiture, the genre o
---

artistic representation in which Figueroa had so 
---
ar specialized.
By 1900, the business o
---
 photographic portraiture was a thriving—even
somewhat saturated—industry in Lima, with little room 
---
or either artistic
experimentation or the bohemian aesthetic that Figueroa had cultivated
in his travels abroad. Some 
---
orty to ﬁ
---
ty studios competed to serve a market made up o
---
 the relatively stable population o
---
 Lima’s oligarchy and
merchant class (see McElroy 1985). These 
---
ashionable studios, which were
ranked in accordance with the prestige and class position o
---
 their respective clienteles, deﬁned a dominant portrait style based on the rigid poses
and stylized theatricality o
---
 the nineteenth-century carte de visite.5
Given the 
---
ormulaic nature o
---
 their composition and content, the ‘‘artistic’’ qualities o
---
 such photographs were ascribed to the varying qualities
o
---
 their sur
---
ace eﬀects. In the early years o
---
 Peruvian photography, when
the prestigious French studios o
---
 Courret, Garreaud, and Manoury dominated Lima’s portrait trade, this aesthetic quality was explained in terms o
---

the technical and lighting eﬀects produced within the print itsel
---
 by the
(European-trained) photographer.6 As photography became more widespread and national, however, the Peruvian photographers—who o
---
ten
could not count on European pedigrees to authorize an artistic status 
---
or
their machine-made images—began to look toward other means o
---
 di
---

---
erentiating the aesthetic value o
---
 their commercial products. The 
---
avored
176

Deborah Poole

2. Figueroa Aznar’s atelier with paintings and work table with 
---
oto-óleos.
Courtesy o
---

Figueroa Aznar, used by permission o

solution was to trans
---
orm totally the machine-produced image into a
hand-produced ‘‘work o
---
 art.’’ This was accomplished through retouching
techniques by which 
---
aces and ﬁgures were altered in the negative and
color added to the ﬁnal print.
O
---
 these retouching techniques, the most radical was that known as

---
otogra
---
ía iluminada (illuminated photography) or 
---
oto-óleo (oil photo) (ﬁg. 2).
In 
---
oto-óleo, oil-based paint was used to idealize the subject’s 
---
eatures, add
color, and even create backdrops and special eﬀects not present in the

experiences on the Yabar

the intellectual and political discourse o
---

the ways in which Figueroa would paint and photograph the Indian.
Indigenismo was a pan-Latin American intellectual movement whose
stated goals were to de
---
end the Indian masses and to construct regionalist
and nationalist political cultures on the basis o
---
 what mestizo, and largely
urban, intellectuals understood to be autochthonous or indigenous cultural 
---
orms. Within this broader vanguard movement, Cusqueño indigenismo occupied a privileged position because o
---
 Cusco’s history as capital o
---
 the Inca Empire. This history made Cusco a particularly contested
site in the battle to wrest a Peruvian national history away 
---
rom the endless scrutiny and historicizing gaze o
---
 the European and North American
scientists, archaeologists, and historians whose expeditionary itineraries
and reports consistently mapped Peruvian history onto that o
---
 a 
---
allen Inca
Empire. In reclaiming Inca history and geography as their own, the Cusqueño indigenistas introduced the previously 
---
orbidden ﬁgure o
---
 the contemporary Andean Indian into the Peruvian literary and artistic imagination,

Although the roots o
---
 Cusco’s indigenista movement can be traced to
colonial and nineteenth-century literature about Indians and the Incas,
what has been called the golden age o
---
 Cusco indigenismo is more use
---
ully
situated in the period 
---
rom 1910 to 1930.29 During this period, indigenista
writing about the Andean countryside, the Inca past, and Indian culture
coalesced with, and responded to, political demands 
---
or greater regional
autonomy and decentralization. These demands in turn responded to the
economic modernization projects and shi
---
ting class alliances o
---
 President
Augusto Leguía’s ‘‘Patria Nueva.’’ Within this context, the most important

---
actor motivating the Cusqueño intellectuals’ interest in the Indian was
a series o
---
 violent peasant uprisings in the mid-1920s. These uprisings in
Cusco’s high pastoral provinces, together with the processes o
---
 economic
modernization occurring in the department’s agrarian valley provinces,
threatened the cultural and social hegemony o
---
 Cusco’s agrarian-based seigneurial class.30 It was in this context that intellectuals 
---
rom Cusco’s upper
and middle classes began their campaign to validate an authentic Indian
identity 
---
or all Cusqueños.
Two major works produced during this period were to determine the

---
uture course o
---
 Cusqueño thinking about the ‘‘Indian problem.’’ These
were Luís Valcárcel’s Tempestad en los Andes, published in 1927, and José Uriel
García’s El nuevo indio, published in 1930 as a rebuttal to Valcárcel’s nationally acclaimed Tempestad. Whereas Valcárcel saw post-Conquest colonial
history as a process o
---
 racial and cultural degeneration and advocated a
return to the values and purity o
---
 pre-Conquest Inca society, García saw
colonial history as a process o
---
 cultural and racial improvement. The true
Indian, he argued, was not pure (or ‘‘Incaic’’) but rather the mixed, or mestizo, product o
---
 Cusco’s colonial past. In con
---
ormance with this historical
vision, García’s indigenista mission was to create a ‘‘New Indian.’’ He rejected
Valcárcel’s vision o
---
 Andean culture as an inherited constant (or received
tradition) that could be resurrected according to the historical or archaeological methodologies o
---
 empirical description and scientiﬁc induction.
Instead, García’s emergent Andean culture was to be a product o
---
 directed
mestizaje (cultural mixture). The New Indian intellectuals who would guide
this mission were to be 
---
orged by melding the spiritual or telluric power
o
---
 the Andean landscape with the intellectual prowess o
---
 a mestizo avantgarde.

188

Deborah Poole

Far 
---
rom being a carbon copy (or much less, imperialist imposition)
o
---
 European modernism, indigenismo was a deliberately iconoclastic pastiche o
---
 philosophical, aesthetic, and discursive borrowings. The result is
a ‘‘modernism’’ that mimics the European only partially and thus appears,

---
rom the point o
---
 view o
---
 European modernism, to have contradictory
structures and purposes.
The most visible contradiction that emerges 
---
rom this process is that
which exists between the Cusqueños’s enthusiastic subscription to the
ideal o
---

---

---
ormalist and internationalist languages o
---

which the modernist avant-garde constituted itsel
---
 in European literature,
painting, photography, and music. For García’s New Indians, modernity,
and in particular the rhythm or velocity o
---
 modern li
---
e, were perceived
as threats to the very landscape 
---
rom which Andean peoples drew their
spiritual and emotional strength. For them, ‘‘indigenismo [was] a return to
the land . . . an instinctive battle against the new concepts o
---
 the Twentieth
Century.’’ 31 Similarly, 
---
or New Indianist artists such as Figueroa, abstraction, 
---
ormalism, and modernity remained taboo.32 As a result, in the eyes o
---

a European, North American, or Limeño observer, most o
---
 the Cusqueño
indigenista paintings appear to be strikingly naive, overly stylized, romantic
compositions; they appear, in short, to be ‘‘bad art.’’
The Cusqueños’s rejection o
---
 modernist pictorial idioms, however, cannot be quite so summarily dismissed. They were both exposed to and
knowledgeable o
---
 modernist pictorial styles. Most were trained in European art. Cubist and other abstract 
---
ormalist styles o
---
 painting were exhibited in Cusco and summarily attacked by the Cusco critics.33 Rather the
Cusqueños’s collective decision not to imitate this style was consciously
made according to two political criteria. On the one hand, it was precisely
the 
---
ormalism and academicism o
---
 European painting that had become
most closely identiﬁed with the hegemonic culture o
---
 Lima. On the other
hand, the political and social legitimacy o
---
 New Indianist discourse remained necessarily situated in a local history and environment. This political and historical grounding in regionalist, anticoastal politics made the
Cusqueños justiﬁably wary o
---
 the European 
---
ormalist aesthetics that celebrated a disconnectedness 
---
rom such historical constants as representation
and tradition.
Figueroa’s approach to painting and photography was molded both by
his personal experiences in Cusco and Paucartambo, and by the ways in

Figueroa Aznar and the Cusco Indigenistas

189

which the indigenista discourses o
---
 Valcárcel and García shaped an understanding o
---
 art, ethnicity, and the intellectual avant-garde that was in many
ways speciﬁc to Cusco. The two decades o
---
 indigenista 
---
erment in Cusco
between 1910 and 1930 correspond to the period when Figueroa was most
active in photography and, although he le
---
t no written records, when indigenista concerns clearly in
---
ormed his studio photos o
---
 idealized Indian
‘‘types’’ (ﬁg. 7), stylized theatrical groups (ﬁg. 8), and bohemian sel
---
-portraits
(ﬁg. 9). In these and other such care
---
ully composed scenes, Figueroa uses
photography to document a constructed artiﬁce o
---
 identity. Each photograph is care
---
ully composed with backdrops painted by Figueroa 
---
or his
theatrical and/or portrait work. In 
---
ront o
---
 these backdrops, an ‘‘Indian’’
model is made to recline, or the artist himsel
---
 poses as stalking hunter,
gypsy minstrel, meditative monk, or pensive artist. In another experiment,
Figueroa skill
---
ully manipulates and composes his negative so as to render
the narrative ﬂow o
---
 a sentiment gained and lost (ﬁg. 10).
This photographic 
---
ascination with the malleability and staging o
---
 social
identities reﬂects a broadening o
---
 Figueroa’s bohemian or walaychu aesthetic to encompass the New Indianists’s philosophy o
---
 constructed cultural identities. Like Figueroa’s various bohemian selves, the New Indianists’s cultural identity, or ‘‘ethnicity,’’ was not a natural or historical identity
waiting to be empirically discovered and described, as in Valcárcel’s indigenismo. Rather, identities were to be constructed according to the consciously elaborated criteria o
---
 a political and artistic avant-garde. Photography ﬁt into this philosophy o
---
 identity or sel
---
 as a means to imagine new
identities, although not as a medium to represent or to express them.
Throughout his li
---
e, Figueroa reserved speciﬁc media 
---
or diﬀerent representational tasks: photography 
---
or sel
---
-portraiture, theater, and type;
painting 
---
or Indians, allegories, and landscapes (ﬁg. 11).34 In the New Indianist conception o
---
 art, painters like Figueroa were to be praised 
---
or the ‘‘realism’’ with which they translated the emotion or beauty inherent to nature
onto the canvas and not 
---
or the ways in which they actively rethought
or imagined new 
---
orms or perspectives on the ‘‘truth’’ o
---
 nature. Photography, on the other hand, was unhampered by its ties to either nature or
art. It could thus be used to document a shi
---
ting and 
---
ormally constructed
notion o
---
 identity more akin to contemporary 
---
orms o
---
 European modernist bohemianism than to the romantic notions o
---
 sentiment, nature,
and art with which the Cusqueños had inscribed and restricted the artist’s
relation to paint.
This aﬃnity between photography and the bohemian sel
---
 was limited,
190

Deborah Poole

however, by the Cusqueños’s doctrinal understandings o
---
 the telluric
qualities o
---
 art in its relation to the land. Uriel García, along with the other
Cusqueño critics, attributed both the sentiment and aesthetic success o
---

Figueroa’s paintings to his immersion in the rural Andean landscape o
---

Paucartambo (where his in-laws owned land).35 They argued that Figueroa’s
special bohemian sensibilities made him a 
---
ertile medium 
---
or translating
onto canvas the spontaneous organic powers that nature had invested in
the Paucartambo landscape and that constituted the essence o
---
 Andean
cultural identity. This aestheticizing or sensitizing eﬀect o
---
 the Andean
landscape was restricted, however, to the medium o
---
 painting and could
not pass through the modernizing mechanical medium o
---
 the camera.
As a result, landscape photography—the ﬁrst pictorialist 
---
orm o
---
 photography to emerge in Europe and the United States—was never seriously
developed in Cusco. Although Cusqueño photographers such as Martín
Chambi, Alberto Ochoa, and Pablo Verdamendi shot extensive photography o
---
 archaeological sites, these photographs were meant to document
the Inca historical past celebrated by indigenista writers such as Valcárcel.36 The task o
---
 expressing the telluric essence o
---
 the landscape itsel
---
 was
meanwhile reserved 
---
or the vanguard o
---
 indigenista artists, who could translate this sentiment into the painterly art o
---
 color, emotion, and personal—
as opposed to mechanical—sensibility.
Other Cusco artists 
---
ollowed Figueroa’s example in the extent to which
they experimented in both photography and painting. With the exception
o
---
 Martín Chambi, however, the indigenista photographers o
---
 Figueroa’s
generation always turned back to painting as the pre
---
erred medium 
---
or expressing what they called el sentimiento andino. Because, 
---
or the indigenistas,
beauty was inherent neither to the Indian nor to the landscape per se, it
could not be captured by what Europeans considered to be the ‘‘magic’’ o
---

photographic technology. Rather, the reality o
---
 the Andean world had to
be trans
---
ormed and reworked, care
---
ully 
---
ramed and skill
---
ully tinted, be
---
ore it could serve as an instrument o
---
 indigenista philosophy and aesthetics. As one indigenista critic expressed it, ‘‘The [indigenista] painters interpret
the Indian landscape and carry pure Indianity to the objectivity o
---
 plastic
arts.’’ 37 Figueroa’s importance in 
---
raming this aesthetic 
---
or both photography and painting is captured in the words o
---
 the indigenista writer José
Gabriel Cosio, whose obituary 
---
or Figueroa lauded him as being ‘‘among
the ﬁrst to employ the indigenista thematic in a purely aesthetic sense with
neither ethnological nor social preoccupations.’’ 38

Conclusions
Four hundred years o
---
 European science has compressed the
expansion o
---
 the original spirit o
---
 our people. For this reason, the
new ideas which are circulating in contemporary thinking must
serve us only as short-term loans or re
---
erence points 
---
or the
aﬃrmation o
---
 our own values.—José Uriel García

By way o
---
 conclusion, I would like to return brieﬂy to my original questions about the universality o
---
 the socially constructed divide between
photography and art, and about Figueroa’s speciﬁc, culturally and socially
situated understanding o
---
 his artistic modernity. As I hope to have made
clear in discussing the origins and 
---
ormation o
---
 the indigenista movement
in which he participated, intellectuals on the ‘‘periphery’’ consciously
borrowed and made use o
---
 the philosophical and aesthetic philosophies
through which, as they well knew, Europe had constructed its hegemonic
discourse o
---
 art and the artist. They did not naively learn the ‘‘neutral’’
194

Deborah Poole

technology o
---
 photography and then use it to record subjects appropriate to their own ‘‘indigenous’’ agenda. Even in 
---
ar-oﬀ ‘‘preindustrial’’ Cusco
the media o
---
 photography and painting were not neutral or transparent
technologies acting simply to reﬂect social ideas and personal inspirations. Rather, as social and aesthetic technologies o
---
 representation, painting and photography in early-twentieth-century Cusco 
---
ormed part o
---

the complex discursive strategies o
---
 Western art. As the Cusco indigenistas themselves knew, they were representational technologies posited
on post-Enlightenment 
---
orms o
---
 Western cultural production in which
the aesthetic representation, classiﬁcation, and idealization o
---
 social types,
natural landscapes, and human 
---
orms had come to 
---
orm an integral part
o
---
 the humanistic philosophies and scientiﬁc knowledge through which
the distribution o
---
 power in society is regulated and controlled.
Because o
---
 its close association with colonial expansion and the globalization o
---
 bourgeois culture in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, photography is a particularly 
---
ascinating medium through which
to study the dialogue through which intellectuals on the periphery contested this ‘‘controlling gaze.’’ The Cusco New Indianists sought to counter
this controlling gaze and positivist inventory o
---
 their history and geography by constructing an ‘‘imagined community’’ based on the philosophical and aesthetic values o
---
 intuition and sentiment in
---
orming their notion
o
---
 la emoción andina.39 Integral to this endeavor was the elaboration o
---
 a
local tradition o
---
 the walaychu, or Andean-bohemian, as a person who replaces the bonds o
---
 social tradition and scientiﬁc history (or pasadismo)
with an aggressive philosophy and aesthetic o
---
 nostalgia, sentiment, and
music grounded in his close ties to the land. Out o
---
 this tradition came
a certain repertoire o
---
 ideas regarding both the bohemian and painting
as 
---
orms o
---
 spontaneous sentimental expression enabled by the telluric

---
orces o
---
 Cusco’s mountainous landscape.
At the same time, however, the emergence o
---
 a pictorial and literary
imagery speciﬁc to the Cusco indigenistas was only enabled by other contemporary developments in European art and in the Latin American vanguardist movement. In Europe, ﬁrst romanticism, with its peasants and
bohemians, and then modernism, with its ‘‘colonial others,’’ emerged as
part o
---
 a broader discursive structure concerned with the elaboration and
reproduction o
---
 social and racial diﬀerence as ‘‘a conscious strategy o
---
 exclusion’’ (Huyssen 1986, vi). This ‘‘strategy o
---
 exclusion’’ made it possible

---
or European artists and writers to engage both industrial culture and the
‘‘colonial other’’ by trans
---
orming that encounter into an aesthetic act o
---

Figueroa Aznar and the Cusco Indigenistas

195

bourgeois cultural production. For the Cusco artist, as well, it was the exclusionary discourse surrounding both the bohemian aesthetic and its successor, vanguard modernism—and not their pictorial or representational
ideologies, which the indigenistas clearly rejected—that enabled the Indian
to sur
---
ace as the subject o
---
 early-twentieth-century Peruvian cultural discourse. This discursive explosion, through which ‘‘the Indian’’ entered the
national artistic—and, somewhat later, political—imagination, was related
to debates in Cusco concerning the desirability o
---
 modernizing the traditional (low-technology, unsalaried) 
---
orms and relations o
---
 production, and
to the increasingly visible political organization and violence o
---
 the Indians themselves. It was this economic and social backdrop o
---
 rapid social
change initiated 
---
rom below that provided the institutional incitement 
---
or
Peruvian intellectuals to speak, ﬁnally, about the previously 
---
orbidden subject o
---
 the Indian.
This discursive shi
---
t, however, was also to have speciﬁc implications 
---
or
the development o
---
 photographic culture in Cusco. Once the hegemonic
conceptual divide between photography (technology) and art had been
surmounted, and the 
---
oto-óleo gone out o
---
 style, the directions in which
Figueroa, and other Cusco artists, would take photography were determined by a new, regionally 
---
ocused discourse on art. As Leguía’s ‘‘Patria
Nueva’’ (1919–1930) championed modernization and North American capital, Cusco politicians and philosophers elaborated an oppositional, regionalist doctrine o
---
 sentiment and antimodernity. In accordance with this new
doctrine, indigenista critics celebrated the individual creativity and ‘‘sentimental realism’’ o
---
 painting 
---
or its ties to nature. They 
---
ocused on both
nature and the Indian as subjects whose essence or ‘‘sentiment’’ could not
be captured by the technological realism (or ‘‘transparency’’) o
---
 photographic representation. What was needed 
---
or the indigenista cultural agenda
was a directed rechanneling o
---
 sentiment and spirit to 
---
orm the newly
imagined community o
---
 ‘‘Cusqueñismo,’’ and not the unmediated realism
o
---
 either photography or the impoverished Indian that photography revealed. Photographic technology and photographic realism were there
---
ore
restricted to the private domain o
---
 photographic ‘‘studies’’ 
---
or both theater
and painting and, in the case o
---
 Figueroa, 
---
or a remarkable series o
---
 sel
---
portraits documenting the indigenista concern with constructing the vanguard or bohemian identities that would someday shape the New Indian
culture o
---
 Cusco.

More precedents exist 
---
or indigenista literature. These include the Cusquena writer Clorinda Matto de Turner, whose novel Aves sin nido (1889) marks
a realist tradition leading up to twentieth-century indigenismo, as well José
Arnaldo Marquez, Mariano Melgar, and Manuel Gonzalez Prada. José Santos
Chocano marks the entry o
---
 the Indian into modernist poetry. The indigenistas o
---
 the 1920s and 1930s diﬀered 
---
rom these predecessors in that they were
the ﬁrst to claim to speak 
---
or contemporary Indians and the ﬁrst to set 
---
orth
a literary and artistic aesthetic based on a notion o
---
 authentic Indian culture (see Delgado 1980 and Polar 1980). Regarding indigenista jurisprudence,
see Poole 1990.
In 1910, the American Alberto Giesecke was appointed rector o
---
 the University o
---
 Cusco. The new curriculum he imposed emphasized European
philosophers such as Spengler, Ortega y Gasset, Leo Froebenius, Taine, and
Georg Simmel. These writers provided the philosophical apparatus with
which the Cusco indigenistas would authorize their arguments about Andean
cultural identity (Herrera 1981, 123–26, and 1980, 187). At the other end o
---
 the
‘‘golden age,’’ 1930 marks the end o
---
 President Augusto Leguía’s dictatorship
(see Herrera 1980; Deustua and Rénique 1984; and Valcárcel 1981).
By the 1920s, Cusco’s land-owning elite was divided both ideologically and
geographically between a modernizing export sector concentrated in the

Bourne’s narrative and images map a space in which the world is, in Heidegger’s sense, a picture. In his 
---

Photography and Sur
---
aces
Contemporary A
---
rican and Indian postcolonial photography is concerned
with a realm o
---

---
ace eﬀects that
operate in a zone o
---

---
rom the detached viewpoint
advocated by early European practitioners such as Bourne. However, I’m
not suggesting that this re

---

is peculiar to photography. On the contrary, as I will show through a number o
---

---

---

---

painterly schemata such as the northern ‘‘art o
---
 describing’’ discussed by
Svetlana Alpers and the ‘‘madness o
---

Such an argument has been made by Martin Jay (1988) in his consideration o
---
 the multiple scopic regimes that have constituted modernity. Jay
argues that the prevailing history o
---
 the dominance o
---
 a single scopic regime—which he calls ‘‘Cartesian perspectivalism’’—is too simple. The term
Cartesian perspectivalism conﬂates Cartesian subjective rationality with
Albertian conceptualizations o
---
 single-point perspective. Central to this
notion is a disembodied vision, what Norman Bryson terms ‘‘vision decarnalised’’ (Bryson 1983, 95) and which I have elsewhere examined (
---
ollowing Susan Buck-Morss) as an ‘‘anaesthetics’’ as opposed to a ‘‘corpothetics.’’1
Within Cartesian perspectivalism, Bryson suggests, ‘‘the body is reduced
to . . . its optical anatomy, the minimal diagram o
---
 monocular perspective.
In the Founding Perception, the gaze o
---
 the painter arrests the ﬂux o
---
 phenomena, contemplates the visual ﬁeld 
---
rom a vantage-point outside the
mobility o
---
 duration, in an eternal moment o
---
 disclosed presence’’ (94). Jay
outlines a number o
---
 related consequences stemming 
---
rom the rise o
---
 this
new visual order. The gap between the viewer and what was represented in
the image widened, and there was a general ‘‘deeroticisation’’ o
---
 the visual
order ( Jay 1988, 8) as the world depicted in images was increasingly ‘‘situated in a mathematically regular spatio-temporal order ﬁlled with natural
objects that could only be observed 
---
rom without’’ (9).
Heidegger’s ‘‘The Age o
---
 the World Picture’’ essay was produced in 1935
—one year be
---
ore Benjamin’s ‘‘Work o
---
 Art’’ essay—and there are certain
intriguing parallels between the two arguments. Both essays develop extraordinarily broad and ambitious evolutionary narratives. In Benjamin’s
optimistic history, a decay o
---
 an earlier situated aura is pressaged by new
technologies o
---
 picturing. In Heidegger’s pessimistic history a positively

208

Christopher Pinney

valorized premodern dwelling is ruptured by Cartesian perspectivalism in
which the world comes to be seen as picture—a zone o
---
 representation
established as something exterior to existence. Picturing becomes inseparable 
---
rom modernity: ‘‘The 
---
act that the world becomes a picture at all is
what distinguishes the modern age’’ (1977, 130).
Whereas 
---
or Parmenides ‘‘man is the one who is looked upon by that
which is’’ in the modern age, ‘‘that which is . . . come[s] into being . . . through
the 
---
act that man ﬁrst looks upon it’’ (1977, 131, 130). Looking upon the world
and constructing the world as picture entails man placing himsel
---
 against
and be
---
ore nature as something separate: the world is ‘‘placed in the realm
o
---
 man’s knowing and o
---
 his having disposal’’ (130).2
Jay opposes the assumption made by commentators as diverse as Richard
Rorty and William Ivins Jr. that Cartesian perspectivalism has been the sole
dominant regime since the Renaissance. He draws attention to other very
diﬀerent paradigms that at times dethroned the supposedly hegemonic
Cartesian perspectival regime. Thus the ‘‘art o
---
 describing,’’ which ﬂourished in the Low Countries during the seventeenth century, rejected the
narrative and perspective o
---
 earlier southern European Renaissance painting in 
---
avor o
---
 denarrativized visual texture. Instead o
---
 a ﬁxed viewing position it demanded a mobile, roving eye, scanning the dispersed details in
the images that 
---
requently include words in addition to objects in its visual
space (1988, 12). Among the contrasts Alpers draws between this art o
---
 describing and earlier perspectival imagery are ‘‘light reﬂected oﬀ objects
versus objects modelled by light and shadow; the sur
---
ace o
---
 objects, their
colours and textures dealt with rather that their placement in a legible
space’’ (1988, 12–13).
The second moment o
---
 unease described by Jay held the possibility o
---

more radical re
---
usal o
---
 perspectivalism. The baroque (a term probably derived 
---
rom the Portuguese word 
---
or a misshapen pearl) is advanced by Jay as
‘‘a permanent, i
---

---
ten repressed, visual possibility throughout the modern
era’’ (1988, 16). Drawing on Christine Buci-Glucksmann’s work, Jay stresses
the baroque’s rejection o
---

---
 the Cartesian tradition, with its illusion o
---
 homogenous three-dimensional space
seen with a God’s-eye view 
---
rom a
---
ar.’’ The baroque is ﬁxated with opacity,
with the recursivity o
---
 sur
---
ace and depth and ‘‘reveals the conventional
rather than the natural quality o
---

---

---

(Pinney 1999) I have discussed popular Indian visual culture in terms o

the baroque as invoked by Alejo Carpentier, who in a 1975 lecture outlined
a theory o

applicability to the images I shall shortly consider.
Although the baroque was, 
---

---

spirit’’ (1995, 93), it needs also to be understood as a reaction to a spatial
rationality. The baroque ‘‘is characterised by a horror o
---

naked sur
---

---

---
rom geometrical arrangements’’ (94). Carpentier sees Bernini’s Saint Peter’s cathedral in Rome as an exemplar o
---

that expands and explores the columns that circumscribe it, that pretend
to demarcate its boundaries and literally disappear be
---
ore its sumptuousness’’ (93). To Carpentier’s stress on the creole and hybrid nature o
---

realism we can add Luis Leal’s observation that magical realism is an ‘‘attitude towards reality’’ (1995, 121) and not just as a literary genre,3 as well as
Amaryll Chanady’s claim that magical realism ‘‘acquires a particular signiﬁcance in the context o
---

and his observation that magical realism is a response to the ‘‘‘rule, norm
and tyranny’ o
---

---

This preoccupation with theoretical perspectives that have evolved out
o

---

that my concern is with photography. What I have tried to demonstrate,
however, is that the ‘‘baroque’’—the appeal to a haptic sur
---
ace—is commonly invoked across diﬀerent media. What all these sur
---
acist strategies
have in common is their emergence in speciﬁc postcolonial contexts as
expressions o
---

---

which Cartesian perspectivalist images are a part.
However, the distance is very slight between these abstract ideas and the
practices o

---

rom the slower-paced rambling 
---
oreground through the building intensity o
---

distant 
---
uture’’ (1982, 17). This image, which is so care
---

permit the viewer’s eye to travel through its internal space, can be con210

Christopher Pinney

trasted with another photograph discussed by Gutman, Women at Sipi Fair

did not know how or where to enter. . . . There were no leads, as you ﬁnd
in Western imagery, to other parts o

The Realist Backdrop
Writing about American Civil War photography, Alan Trachtenberg has
noted what he calls ‘‘historicism-by-photography,’’ the notion—in the
wake o
---
 the consecration o
---
 photography by war—that ‘‘historical knowledge declares its true value by its photographability’’ (1985, 1). Perhaps the
clearest statement o
---
 this trans
---
ormation in the nature o
---
 historical events
lies in Paul Valéry’s conclusion, a hundred years a
---
ter photography’s introduction, that ‘‘the mere notion o
---
 photography, when we introduce it into
our meditation on the genesis o
---
 historical knowledge and its true value,
suggests the simple question: Could such and such a 
---
act, as it is narrated, have
been photographed?’’ (Valéry, cited in Trachtenberg 1989, xiii–xiv).
Valéry’s observation expresses a standard view o
---
 photography as indexical—as a chemical trace o
---
 the light bounced oﬀ objects passed be
---
ore the
lens—and as having an element o
---

---
rame and construct the image. It is the incompleteness o
---
 the photographic ﬁlter that sets it apart 
---
rom other media, such as
painting and drawing. Implicit in this view is a desire 
---
or imprisonment
within the time space o
---
 the everyday world, captured by the camera.
Photography can be used to position bodies and 
---
aces within history,
and it can also be used as a means o
---
 escape. What Eduardo Cadava (1997,
xxix) terms ‘‘photographic sel
---
-archiving’’ positions bodies within speciﬁc
chronotopes—it matters to some people when you went to France that
the trace o
---
 the Eiﬀel Tower in the distance is the one in the center o
---

Paris and not a simulacrum: as Bourdieu says, it ‘‘consecrates the unique
encounter’’ (1990, 36). The central Indian chronotope I now want brieﬂy
to describe doesn’t privilege these 
---
actors because its ludic photographic
idiom is not concerned with ﬁxing bodies in particular times and places;
rather it is concerned with the body as a sur
---
ace that is completely mutable
and mobile, capable o
---
 being situated in any time and space.
Whereas in some photographic traditions, backdrops are valued as a
record o
---
 the subject’s position in a particular actual space (usually, as

The ‘‘Subaltern’’ Backdrop
Writing in A
---
terimage, the anthropologist Arjun Appadurai has suggested
that the widespread use o
---

photography expresses a resistance to the documentary claims o
---

---
oregrounding o
---
 critiques o
---
 modernity. Appadurai goes so

---
ar as to label the backdrop ‘‘subaltern,’’ where ‘‘the backdrop resists, subverts or parodies the realist claims o
---
 photography in various ways. . . .
In these postcolonial settings, photographic backdrops become less the
site 
---
or debates about colonial subjectivity and more the place 
---
or . . . ‘experiments with modernity.’ That is, outside the taxonomising and coercive
techniques o
---

become part o
---

---

---
e’’ (1997, 5).
In Nagda, backgrounds are used not simply as a substitute in the absence
o
---

---

studios also 
---

---

o

---

Double and triple portraits place a person beyond the space and identity that certain 
---

also suggests a diﬀerent conceptualization o
---

spatial and temporal in
