‘‘Coming Out Better . . .’’
No one in Nagda—apart, that is, 
---
rom the police and other agents o
---
 the
state—sees any value in photography’s potential to ﬁx quotidian reality. It
is rarely there to record or to memorialize the events and conjunctions
o
---
 the past. It is prized, rather, 
---
or its ability to make people and places
‘‘come out better than they really are.’’ 4 This is a phrase used by customers
o
---
 Nagda’s Sagar Studio, which is 
---
amous 
---
or its double color exposures.
The proprietor, Vijay Vyas, observes that Nagdarites rarely desire ‘‘realistic’’
(vastavik) photographs: ‘‘They always say I want to look good. . . . Everyone
says I am like this but I want to come out better than this in my photo [is
se bhi zyada acchha mera photo ana chahie]. So we try.’’
‘‘Coming out better’’ in a Nagda photograph is achieved through two
routes: through the adoption o
---
 gestures and through the deployment o
---

costume and props. Frequently the one implies the other. Vijay Vyas re
---
ers
to any photo involving a gesture with the English phrase ‘‘action shooting’’:
‘‘action shooting or an action photo means you’ve got your hand in a certain
way, holding it up to show your watch, one leg is higher than another, these
are action photos.’’ This re
---
ers to a set o
---
 techniques agreed on between
the sitter and the photographer that allow a particular pose to emerge and
that may jointly involve a gesture or look by the subject and the adoption
o
---
 an appropriate camera angle by the photographer. Thus there are ‘‘poet’’
and ‘‘ﬁlmi’’ (i.e., ﬁlm star) poses that both require low camera angles, and
the creation o
---
 these involves a sort o
---
 theatrical direction by the photographer (ﬁg. 2).
Vijay, like several other Nagda studio owners, also has a collection o
---

costumes available 
---
or the use o
---
 his clients, although they are the cause
o
---
 considerable tribulations. The costumes include an all-in-one dress that
can be arranged to give diﬀerent regional, caste, and class ﬂavors. I asked
Vijay why his clients are so keen to wear diﬀerent clothes when they are
in 
---
ront o
---
 the camera: ‘‘For shauq [pleasure, to satis
---
y a desire]. They don’t
want to wear their everyday clothes. They don’t want vastavik [realistic]
photos. . . . They brush and braid their hair, put on [talcum] powder, put
on cream, put on lipstick, they change their dress, they wear good clothes,

Christopher Pinney

2. Composite print by Suhag Studios, Nagda, c. 1980.

put on a tie, wear a coat, they make sure their trousers are ok, then they
say ‘take an action photo,’ ‘take my photo with this type o
---

dress, with a matka [earthenware pot]’ and so on.’’
Whereas Nagda popular photography does not seem to share much
with the solemnizing 
---

---

pictorial space, and through lighting, depth o
---

---

---

and expression thus reveal a ‘sel
---

more than what we really are’ ’’ (1995 n.p.). The comment at the end is by
Seydou Keïta himsel

Vernacular Modernism
Coming 
---
rom an immersion in popular Indian photographic practices,
one is immediately struck by similarities with documented studies o
---

popular A
---
rican photography: there appears to be a similar disinterest in
realist chronotopes, a similar re
---
usal o
---
 Cartesian perspectivalism, even
as it exists in its attenuated contemporary Euro-American popular incarnations. Some o
---
 the apparent continuities can be in part explained by a
continuity o
---

paper in this volume) in Mombasa are largely Indian run, and the power
---
ul
inﬂuence o
---
 Bombay cinema throughout West A
---
rica (Larkin 1997) and the
impact o
---

possible lineages o
---
 inﬂuence.
The work o
---
 Seydou Keïta (whose championing by the art world as the
key A
---
rican photographer could be the subject o
---
 another essay) is particularly resonant with popular Indian portraiture. Seydou Keïta’s clients
had, however, a much more restricted choice o
---
 backdrops. As Seydou reported to the Guardian on 19 April 1997, ‘‘Between 1949 and 1952, I used my

---
ringed bedspread as my ﬁrst backdrop. Then I changed the background
every two or three years.’’ In another source, he adds that this introduced
an element o
---
 indeterminacy: ‘‘Sometimes the background went well with
their clothes, especially 
---
or the women, but it was all haphazard’’ (Keïta,
cited in Bigham 1999, 58).
These sporadically changed backdrops contribute signiﬁcantly to the
Seydou look—and to their re
---
usal o
---
 the realist chronotope. Any location
outside o
---
 the imaginary space o
---
 the studio is continually exceeded by the
texture o
---
 Seydou’s various bedspreads entering into harmonious and dissonant conversation with the clothes and accessories worn by his sitters
(ﬁg. 3). This conjunction produces a Malian ‘‘art o
---
 describing,’’ a photographic sur
---
acism that engages with texture, where everything springs out
o
---
 the photograph toward the viewer, rather than a ﬁeld o
---
 spatio-temporal
certainty receding within the image.5 This is what we might label ‘‘vernacular modernism’’: a re
---
usal o
---
 external veriﬁcation prompted not by Greenbergian angst but by a desire to consolidate the intimate space between
viewer and image.
The most popular o
---

---
rom the 1950s, perhaps because their subjects radiate the optimism o
---
 the winds o
---
 change o
---
 an
imagined 
---
uture (ﬁg. 4), rather than the weariness o
---
 Mbembesque postcolonial ‘‘banality’’ (Mbembe 1992). The material discussed by Stephen

o
---

o
---

---
ace. A shallow picture space, collage and montage techniques, overpainting, and complex sculptural mediations transpose the

---
rom the space between the image’s window and its re

---
ace and their beholders. It is this

these notes 
---
rom the sur
---
ace o
---

Imagined Journeys
TH E LIKON I F E RRY P H OTOGRAPHERS
OF MOMB A S A , K ENYA

T

here exists a longstanding association between travel and vision. However, in Europe be
---
ore 1600, the aristocratic traveler went abroad
mainly 
---
or ‘‘discourse’’—to involve the reﬂective and disciplined exercise
o
---
 the ear and the tongue, rather than 
---
or picturesque views (Adler 1989, 9).
In the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries, in travel sermons and manuals, the eye slowly gained superiority over the ear. At this time, tourists
carried Claude glasses, brownish convex mirrors named 
---
or the painter
Claude Lorrain, which reﬂected condensed images o
---
 landscapes. With
the help o
---
 the glass, they trans
---
ormed the landscape they were traveling
through into images o
---
ten 
---
ollowing the picturesque conventions o
---
 landscape painting. The Claude glass can be seen as a predecessor o
---
 the camera.
As a technical instrument it privileged as well as mediated the gaze and
sight and structured travel experience. Thus, touristic perception created
its own imagined spaces (Wagner 1991, 330; Hennig 1997, 53).
Three months a
---
ter the camera had been invented and presented to the
Academy o
---
 Science in Paris in 1839, the ﬁrst daguerreotypists reached
Cairo in A
---
rica and started ‘‘daguerreotyping like lions’’ (H. Vernet, cited
in Howe 1994). A 
---
ew years later, when Thomas Cook began organizing
mass tourism in 1845, photographs oﬀered indisputable evidence that the
trip had been made. By taking pictures o
---
 pyramids and temples, tourists

appropriated the things photographed and put themselves into a knowledgeable and power
---
ul relation to the world. Photographs helped them to
take possession o
---
 space in which they might have 
---
elt insecure (Sontag
1979, 4, 9). However, when the distance between tourists and Egyptian li
---
e
grew, photographic representation came to provide a simulacrum o
---
 the
experiences they had helped to avoid. Staged commercial photographs recapitulated exotic street scenes (Howe 1994, 41). Thus, since the invention
o
---
 photography, a highly complex and symbiotic relationship has grown up
between it and the activity o
---
 travel; photographic representations became
inseparable 
---
rom actual journey, the camera mediating the experience o
---

travel. Western travelers used photographs, on the one hand, to produce
‘‘
---
aith
---
ul pictorial records’’ by employing the camera as an instrument o
---

evidence, thereby rein
---
orcing the ‘‘culture o
---
 realism’’ (Green-Lewis 1996,
31), while photographic practices, on the other hand, paradoxically also
subverted this realism. The commercial photographic studios that were
built up at the turn o
---
 the twentieth century in various A
---
rican towns produced a space o
---
 wish 
---
ulﬁllment and illusions that transcended ‘‘the culture o
---
 realism’’ while at the same time conﬁrming it.
In the 
---
ollowing I would like to present a group o
---
 young photographers
in Mombasa, Kenya, who have made their studios the sites o
---
 a visual recycling o
---
 images produced by modern mass media. By localizing as well
as domesticating the global ﬂow o
---
 images, they have created a new space
o
---
 wish 
---
ulﬁllment and imagination that allows their customers to participate in a world 
---
rom which in actuality they are excluded.

The Likoni Ferry Photographers
In the course o
---
 my research on popular photography in A
---
rica, I became
acquainted with a group o
---
 young photographers who called themselves
the Likoni 
---
erry photographers.1 At the beginning o
---
 the 1990s, they settled
in Likoni, a quarter o
---
 Mombasa, on a steep bluﬀ directly on the shore,
opposite Old Town.
Already in the 1930s, Mombasa, an old Swahili town, became a place o
---

particular attraction 
---
or white settlers. The sea that surrounds the island,
as well as the island’s creeks, provided immense pleasure 
---
or the devotees
o
---
 deep-sea angling, sailing, and—later—water skiing. Numerous sports
clubs were established and created an atmosphere o
---
 ‘‘sea level and sanity’’
( Jewell 1976, xiii). During this time, white settler tourism singled out and
222

Heike Behrend

demarcated certain privileged spaces—the beach, the sea, and certain
buildings in Old Town—
---
rom which A
---
ricans were excluded. Space was
regionalized into diﬀerent zones occupied by diﬀerent classes o
---
 people.
Pictures o
---
 these privileged places started circulating, rein
---
orming and rein
---
orcing certain images.
In the 1960s, the 
---
orces o
---
 new technology and globalization processes allowed Western mass tourism to invade the coast. During the 1980s, Kenya’s
tourism industry was the country’s number-one 
---
oreign exchange earner
and one o
---
 the largest employers. Western mass tourism turned certain
sites and landscapes into commodities and invested them with new meanings. Landscape, in its double role as commodity and potent cultural symbol, came to be presented and re-presented in ‘‘packaged tours’’: an object
to be purchased, consumed, and even brought home in the 
---
orm o
---
 souvenirs, such as postcards and photographs (Mitchell 1994a, 15).
This tourist boom attracted a large number o
---
 migrant workers, who
came to the coast to work in tourist hotels, at the harbor, in game parks,
or as photographers. Although the tourist trade has decreased strongly
in recent years, migrant workers still travel to the coast to look 
---
or various opportunities. Most o
---
 the Likoni photographers came to Mombasa
as migrant workers 
---
rom Central Province or western Kenya. Strangers
in Mombasa themselves, they recruited their customers 
---
rom the pool o
---

migrant labor and A
---
rican tourists, people 
---
rom up-country, whom the
local city people contemptuously re
---
erred to as washamba—that is, peasants or ﬁeld workers. On weekends and holidays many o
---
 these migrant
laborers turned into tourists. O
---
ten they were visited by 
---
amily members

---
rom up-country, and together with their relatives they went to see the
Indian Ocean as well as the sites o
---
 Mombasa Old Town, and, as proo
---
 that
they had been there, to have themselves photographed. As a photographer,
Mr. Maina Hutchinson, explained to me, the ‘‘clic-clic culture’’ o
---
 Western tourists had reached A
---
rica. Like Western tourists, who were ‘‘crazy’’
about taking pictures o
---
 every site they visited, Kenyans now also wanted
to record everything in photographs, and today photography is an integral part o
---
 local A
---
rican tourism. The Likoni 
---
erry photographers proﬁted

---
rom this trend, and they contributed substantially to molding and simultaneously trans
---
orming the conventions o
---
 this tourism.
Figure 1 shows three young migrant workers who returned to their
homes up-country a
---
ter the Likoni violence in August and September
1997.2 They wanted to be photographed together with the camera, which
is placed in the center in the position o
---
 greatest importance. Moreover,

Most o
---
 the Likoni photographers began as street photographers and
then slowly, with little equipment, built up their studios (although without electricity and running water).3 They called themselves jua kali, which
is Swahili 
---
or ‘‘hot or sharp sun’’ and designates people who work in the ‘‘in
---
ormal sector.’’ Thus, in their small, mobile studios, they occupy an intermediate position between the established studios and the street photographers (ﬁgs. 2 and 3).
All told, about thirty photographers worked in Likoni, ten o
---
 which had
their own studios and the rest, among them six young women, worked
as roving photographers down by the dock or sometimes as touts 
---
or the
studios. At the beginning, women worked only in subordinate positions,
Imagined Journeys

225

but when I returned to Likoni in April 2000 three women had managed to
establish their own studios.
These young men and women were autodidacts or, as they themselves
proudly put it, sel
---
-made men and women. They came 
---
rom the ranks o
---

those largely excluded 
---
rom social and economic opportunities, and there
was a strong 
---
eeling o
---
 marginalization and insecurity among them. It is
not by chance, I suppose, that with a 
---
ew exceptions they all belonged to
various independent 
---
undamentalist Christian churches, which demand
o
---
 their members a radical rupture with their (sin
---
ul) past while at the same
time promising them salvation and economic success in this world.
In my conversations with these photographers they emphasized the rivalry among themselves. They explained that they produced the diﬀering
decorations in their studios in opposition to each other—although this
didn’t prevent them 
---
rom imitating or adopting a competitor’s attractive
decor. Their goal was to attract customers, and, 
---
or business reasons, each
tried to outdo the others. They didn’t see their decor as art but as an aspect o
---
 commerce or business. An installation was good and beauti
---
ul i
---

it brought customers; their own creativity 
---
ollowed the logic o
---
 capitalistic competition and the law o
---
 accelerated innovation. Accordingly, they
changed the backdrops and equipment o
---
 their studios 
---
requently. They
saw themselves as modern because, as one photographer explained, they
always knew how to oﬀer the newest things.
These photographers worked more or less illegally as squatters because
the land occupied by their studios belonged to the city or to the 
---
erry
company. I believe that this illegal status contributed substantially to the
particular aesthetic o
---
 the studios. The state rigidly prevents other street
photographers, who work on public squares, 
---
rom creatively altering these
sites; as one street photographer put it, these sites must remain ‘‘natural.’’
But the Likoni 
---
erry photographers were not subject to this degree o
---
 state
restrictions and sanctions. Their existence was repeatedly threatened and
they had to 
---
ear being driven away, but their studios enjoyed ‘‘artistic 
---
reedom.’’ Within the postcolonial Kenyan state, the photographers were able
to occupy a space that eluded the state authorities. Indeed, the Likoni 
---
erry
photographers and their productions were a unique local development
that made use o
---
 a great variety o
---
 global elements. As Daniel Miller (1995)
describes, here we have an innovation, a discontinuity produced, so to
speak, a posteriori out o
---
 the local consumption o
---
 global elements.

226

Heike Behrend

Remote Places
In their studios, the Likoni photographers created environments that made
use o
---
 the immense reservoir o
---
 images available in Mombasa. In their
decor they produced assemblages that singled out as well as juxtaposed
pictures o
---
 various places all over the world. Exotic tapestries made in
Turkey (reaching Kenya via Dubai) showing sublime Alpine landscapes or
play
---
ul horses in splashing water, wild tigers, roaring stags, Jesus as shepherd, the Last Supper, or the Kaaba o
---
 Mecca, as well as paintings picturing
airplanes, highways, and luxury steamliners, were brought together with
a homely so
---
a suggesting a modern middle-class salon or sitting room.
As I have shown elsewhere in more detail (Behrend 2000), by juxtaposing
and mixing the images o
---
 various places the Likoni photographers, as migrant workers, detached themselves 
---
rom the logic o
---
 a ﬁxed place, thereby
dissolving the separation between ‘‘here’’ and ‘‘there’’ by being global. In
their studios, they realized the vision o
---
 ‘‘the world-in-the-home’’ and ‘‘the
home-in-the-world’’ (Bhabha 1994, 141).
In their studios, the photographers oﬀered their customers the chance to
enter a space 
---
rom which in actuality they are excluded. Customers could
trans
---
orm themselves into airplane passengers, part o
---
 the modern jet set,
and thus connected to a wider global world (ﬁgs. 4 and 5). In the photos,
the photographed persons are placed in an airport as a privileged place, a
‘‘non-site,’’ in the sense that it is a place o
---
 arriving and departing, a place
o
---
 passage not o
---
 staying (ﬁgs. 6 and 7). Through the photographs, the depicted persons participate in the increasing acceleration and mobility o
---

modern li
---
e, shi
---
ting boundaries and thus producing a cosmopolitan visualized discourse o
---
 potentially being everywhere. In this context it is not
by chance that Mr. Jay Ngao, the owner o
---
 Jay’s Studio, recently changed
the name o
---
 his studio to Global Studio. Mr. Jay Ngao explained to me that
most o
---
 the customers 
---
rom up-country did not know ships; 
---
or them the
sea and ships 
---
ormed part o
---
 a 
---
oreign exotic landscape that attracted them,
and that this is why, among the Likoni studio owners, painted backdrops
with luxury liners proli
---
erated (ﬁgs. 8 and 9).
In the Francis Studio, ironically, the Titanic became a very success
---
ul
backdrop to celebrate the millennium. As I was told, it was, above all, the
recently produced ﬁlm with the same title that made the ship an icon o
---
 a
(catastrophic) modernity (ﬁg. 10).
In Mr. Jay Ngao’s Studio in 1996 and in the Omalla Studio in 1998 there
were backdrops that showed two sites o
---
 Nairobi: the Kenyatta Con
---
erence

When I asked Mr. Jay Ngao whether migrant workers originating in the
Central Province, 
---
rom Nairobi, came to be photographed at his studio because they were homesick and wanted to remember their native place, he
answered in the negative. Instead, he said, people 
---
rom the coast who never
had been to the capital would come to his studio to take a picture that
allowed them to be in Nairobi. Thus, the Likoni photographers not only
used the exotic, more or less 
---

---

well as other parts o
---

---

studios, they and their customers would travel to various places all over the
world (compare Pinney 1997a, 175). While the photographers as well as their
customers could not aﬀord to travel to Nairobi by airplane or to board a
luxury liner, in the studios they did. Here, modern means o
---
 transport and
exotic places were at their disposal, and the studios with their imaginative
geographies became a surrogate 
---
or travel.

Wildli

e
In March 1998, when I again visited Likoni, the photographers had developed some new backdrops, this time re
---

wildli
---
e (ﬁgs. 13 and 14).
In their painted backdrops, they reproduced touristic pictures o
---
 an
exotic, idyllic, naturalized A
---
rican wilderness that originally had been produced as a counterimage to industrial capitalism, and had thus become the
imaginary repository o
---
 value 
---
orms lost in the process o
---
 modernization
(Bunn 1996, 38). The photographers also appropriated certain aspects o
---
 the
‘‘hunting tradition,’’ and its cheaper variant o
---
 the nonhunting tours to the
game parks, via existing touristic conventions. However, they ‘‘modernized’’ the images o
---
 a primordial wilderness by juxtaposing modern technology, such as an airplane, in the sky. Thus, in Likoni, the hegemonic space
that belonged 
---
or some time more or less exclusively to Western tourists
was appropriated and trans
---
ormed into the vision o
---
 an A
---
rican modernity.

Touristic Sites o
---
 Mombasa
In addition to sites in Nairobi and other parts o
---
 the world, the Likoni photographers in their studios also re
---
erred to certain places o
---
 their exile, o
---

Mombasa—all o
---
 which were related to tourism (ﬁg. 15).
The backdrops in ﬁgure 15 show Fort Jesus, a 
---
ortress built in 1593 by the
Portuguese, and thus one o
---
 the oldest buildings on the coast. As its past
history has shown, the island o
---
 Mombasa was at the mercy o
---
 whoever
had command o
---
 Fort Jesus (Gray 1957, 49). Consequently, when the British
protectorate was proclaimed, the 
---
ort was converted into a prison. In 1958
the building was declared a historical monument and trans
---
ormed into a
museum. Although originally constructed by Portuguese colonialists, the

---
ort ﬁnally became a site o
---
 cultural value and a symbol o
---
 the Kenyan postcolonial nation—a symbol that was and still is reproduced in every school
book.4 Having been trans
---
ormed into a national museum, Fort Jesus became one o
---
 the most important ‘‘attractions.’’ With the 
---
ort’s sound and
light show three nights a week, it 
---
orms part o
---
 a ‘‘Mombasa by Night’’
package designed especially 
---
or Western tourists.
Another site the Likoni photographers depicted in their backdrops was
the ‘‘Tusks’’ which, in addition to other triumphal arches, were put up in
1956 
---
or Princess Margaret’s visit to Mombasa. The idea came 
---
rom the ten
234

Heike Behrend

15. Simon Expert Studio, 1995.

16. Jay’s Studio, 1998.

cent coin, which had tusks on the obverse side, and was suggested by the
treasurer o
---
 the town council. Two large pairs o
---
 tusks were constructed
out o
---
 canvas on wooden 
---
rames by the municipal engineer’s department
and erected on Kilindini Road (now Moi Avenue). Such was the impression they made that the council decided they should become a permanent 
---
eature o
---
 the town. They were reconstructed in aluminum and put
in their present position.5 Today, some people without housing use them
as shelters during the night. The tusks became a landmark o
---
 Mombasa
and a 
---
avorite tourist site, and their picture widely circulates on postcards
(ﬁg. 16).
In addition, the Likoni photographers produced picturesque images o
---

the sea, the beach, and palm trees, spaces that originally were occupied by
and reserved 
---
or Western tourists. These images also 
---
ollowed the genre
conventions o
---
 seaside paintings 
---
or tourists, above all postcards showing
idyllic, untouched beaches, palm trees, and the blue sea. Thus, the Likoni
photographers took over the basic principles o
---
 tourist art: the masking o
---

speciﬁc cultural and political re
---
erents in an eﬀort to broaden the popular
appeal o
---
 a place and thereby create a larger audience ( Jules-Rosette 1995,
43). They oﬀered stereotyped, depoliticized souvenir images o
---
 an exotic
landscape, sometimes even cleansed o
---
 A
---
rican as well as Western tourists or inhabitants. Landscape here is thus a 
---
orm o
---
 imaginary resolution
or ideological naturalization, because the problematic issues o
---
 labor and
land tenure are veiled (Bunn 1993, 50).
In their decor, however, the Likoni photographers reproduced not only
the places o
---
 interest 
---
or Western tourists but also the Western tourist’s
gaze—the gaze 
---
rom outside that took possession o
---
 A
---
rican places and
landscapes. Their visual agendas were strongly deﬁned by the hegemonic
tourist conventions. However, in appropriating in their photographs sites
and landscapes that 
---
ormerly were reserved 
---
or Western tourists, they reversed the processes o
---
 dispossession and exclusion. In addition, by positioning themselves as subjects in these touristic landscapes, they not only
subverted the hegemonic conventions but also related to themselves as
subjects o
---
 aesthetic experience (Battaglia 1995, 87). Furthermore, although
Western tourist conventions single out sites and places as unique or as cultural markers o
---
 speciﬁc spaces, the Likoni photographers in their studios
not only juxtaposed but piled up images o
---
 various places into a bricolage o
---
 a global world that is thereby decentered. In their ‘‘wild’’ assemblages, they dehierarchized the cultural values attached to various places,
thus subverting the hegemonic Western discourse. I think it possible to
236

Heike Behrend

17. Sammy Big Seven Studio, 1996.

view their assemblages as a critical comment on representation because
the representation in their montages is never complete in itsel
---
; instead, its
eﬀect lies in juxtaposing a heightened sense o
---
 reality with one o
---
 
---
antasy,
thereby encouraging spectators and customers to speculate on representation itsel
---
 (Marcus 1995, 47).

Truth and Fiction
When I asked the Likoni photographers why so many o
---
 their customers
pre
---
erred to be photographed be
---
ore painted backdrops showing, 
---
or example, the sea, instead o
---
 having pictures taken in 
---
ront o
---
 the actual Indian
Ocean, they explained that many o
---
 their customers pre
---
erred the painted
backdrops ‘‘because they were more beauti
---
ul.’’ They gave the example o
---

the 
---
erry connecting Likoni with the island or Old Town o
---
 Mombasa that
in reality was old, rusty, and dirty, whereas as a backdrop it looked nice
and clean (ﬁg. 17).
In their images, the Likoni photographers did not so much attempt to
produce an outside ‘‘reality’’ but rather they used the decor o
---
 their studios
to improve the world and to trans
---
orm and upgrade their own position in
Imagined Journeys

237

it. For them, the painted backdrops were ‘‘real’’; they were not part o
---
 attempts to deceive or lie, although they did tell me that some customers
would cheat and send photos to their relatives at home claiming that they
had been at the airport or used a luxury liner. When I talked to the painter
Mr. Masada, 
---
rom whom the photographers ordered their backdrops, I

---
ound out that he extensively used photographs 
---
rom journals, books, or
calendars, 
---
rom which he copied his pictures. Mr. Masada explained to me
that as a painter he needed to have a camera. Thus, painting and photography were not so much seen as diﬀerent genres or techniques o
---
 truth
production, but rather 
---
ormed phases in a more general process o
---
 producing images: in this process photographs were changed into paintings
and paintings again into photographs, all o
---
 them deserving the status o
---

representing ‘‘reality.’’
To return to the beginning o
---
 this essay: in their studios, the Likoni photographers produced a space o
---
 wish 
---
ulﬁllment, illusions, and imaginations that transcended ‘‘the culture o
---
 realism’’ while at the same time conﬁrming it. By taking photographs o
---
 painted backdrops that had previously
been photographs, they took part in the production o
---
 a new regime o
---
 ﬁction that molds the West as well as A
---
rica: in this regime, it is not so much
the world outside that is real; instead, the images have now gained reality
(Flusser 1997, 349).

Yoruba Photography
H OW TH E YORU B A S E E THEM SELV ES

P

hotographers and photographic studios are prevalent throughout
many areas o
---
 A
---
rica today, and particularly in West A
---
rica many indigenous societies make use o
---
 this medium. The Yoruba, though not
unique, are certainly exceptional in the extent to which they have integrated photography into many contemporary and traditional aspects o
---

their culture. Some Yoruba photographs reﬂect traditional cultural values,
and some are even utilized in certain traditional rituals (ﬁg. 1). These more
conventionalized 
---
orms o
---
 photography seem to exist in the smaller, more
isolated Yoruba towns and villages. In the large cities such as Ibadan and
Lagos, where contemporary Western photography is more inﬂuential, the
work exhibits a greater sophistication and a wider range o
---
 styles and techniques.
Photography is no newcomer to A
---
rica. Three months a
---
ter Daguerre
publicly announced his daguerreotype process in 1839, the French romantic painter Horace Vernet wrote 
---
rom Alexandria, Egypt, ‘‘We keep daguerreotyping like lions, and 
---
rom Cairo hope to send home an interesting
batch’’ (Bensusan 1966, 7). In colonial South and East A
---
rica, the ﬁrst studios
were opened in the 1840s (11), and photography immediately became a part
o
---
 colonial li
---
e. In West A
---
rica it is likely that photography was introduced
more slowly and coincided with the later nineteenth-century expansion
o
---
 permanent European colonies and political rule. It was past 1900, 
---
or instance, be
---
ore the interior o
---
 Nigeria was completely under British rule,

1. This chie
---
 and Babalawo
(I
---
a diviner) requested that

amily set up

ore some o

made (82).
Considering the long history o
---
 photography in colonial A
---
rica, it seems
reasonable to assume that many indigenous societies would have had
ample opportunity to become 
---
amiliar with the medium and to make it as
much a part o
---
 their own cultures as seemed appropriate. Indigenous photographers have indeed been working in West A
---
rica at least since the 1930s
and probably very much earlier, as their actual existence would likely predate by a considerable number o
---
 years their recognition and documentation in the literature. A picture by a Gold Coast photographer appeared in a
pre–World War II British book on commercial photography with the condescending comment that ‘‘crude though the result it had virtues which
showed that the mind o
---
 the photographer was at work’’ (Charles 1938, 17).
A possibly much earlier example is the large 
---
ramed photograph hanging over the doorway to a traditional abiku 1 shrine in the Yoruba town o
---

Ila-Orangun. It depicts the mother o
---
 the old priestess who is presently
head o
---
 the society, and it could date 
---
rom as early as pre–World War I.

chie

Most o

Yoruba photography in Ila-Orangun and elsewhere is, almost exclusively,
posed portraiture o
---

---
 people, which are o
---
ten commissioned in order to commemorate an event o
---
 some importance to those
depicted. Though an important ritual object or prized possession, such as
a traditional sculpture or a new car, might occasionally be photographed,
general subjects such as landscapes, architecture, or ordinary objects and
events are seldom taken by local photographers.3 In short, the Yoruba have
developed unstated but clearly discernible conventions regarding the apYoruba Photography

243

propriate subject matter; those governing posing and presentation o
---
 the
subject matter have also developed. Together, these conventions oﬀer a
codiﬁcation o
---
 a range o
---
 Yoruba cultural values.4
Yoruba photography certainly shares similar categories o
---
 subject matter and 
---
ormalistic conventions with other West A
---
rican societies and
with Western cultures, particularly British culture. But cultural patterning
exists, not in subtle diﬀerences in these conventions but more importantly
in the unique, culturally derived symbolic meanings and speciﬁc 
---
unctions
attributed to these seemingly similar 
---
orms. It is beyond the scope o
---

essay to analyze all the diverse external and indigenous 
---
actors that might
have contributed to the development o
---

simply seek here to establish Yoruba photography as a genuine expression
o
---
 the culture by discussing several speciﬁc examples with respect to how
their subject matter, 
---
ormal and stylistic conventions, symbolic meanings,
and 
---
unction within Yoruba society are in part a reﬂection o
---

---
 the world.
Older Yoruba in traditional dress o
---
ten sit in a particular stylized manner at traditional ceremonies and events. This pose has become codiﬁed
usually through photography and now seems to be the accepted way 
---
or
traditional Yoruba to present themselves in a 
---
ormal photographic portrait. What I call the ‘‘traditional 
---
ormal portrait’’ is highly conventionalized both in the manner in which the subject poses and the manner in
which the photograph is composed (see ﬁgs. 1 and 2). The subject always
wears his best traditional dress and sits squarely 
---
acing the camera. Both
hands are placed on the lap or on the knees, and the legs are well apart
to spread the garments and display the 
---
abrics. The 
---
ace has a digniﬁed
but distant expression as the eyes look directly at and through the camera. Symbols o
---
 the subject’s position in Yoruba society are worn, held, or
placed conspicuously nearby (ﬁg. 3). The photographer enhances the sense
o
---
 digniﬁed stateliness by a camera viewpoint either level with the subject’s waist or looking slightly upward, as i
---
 
---
rom the position o
---
 one paying
homage. The entire body is always included within the 
---
rame, and a neutral vertical background immediately behind serves to isolate the subject
in a shallow three-dimensional space. The ﬁgure gives a 
---
eeling o
---
 sculptural massiveness and solidarity, and the whole pose is one o
---
 symmetry
and balance.5
It might be argued that the traditional 
---
ormal portrait is in part a synthesis o
---
 certain traditional Yoruba cultural values, the inherent attributes o
---

nineteenth-century photography and nineteenth-century British attitudes

I
---

ten visually interacts with the

Both nineteenth-century British and traditional Yoruba cultures placed
great emphasis on tradition, proper conduct, and the identity and maintenance o
---
 one’s social position. Early British portraits and Yoruba traditional

---
ormal portraits visually codi
---
y these commonly held values: the digniﬁed
pose, proper clothes, and o
---
ten the display o
---

---

subject’s pro

---

the traditional 
---
ormal portrait emphasizes how well the subject 
---
ulﬁlls his
traditional role in Yoruba society.
The traditional 
---
ormal portrait, o
---
 all the 
---
orms o
---
 Yoruba photography, most clearly embodies in its composition not only certain traditional Yoruba cultural values, but also their aesthetic values as outlined
by R. F. Thompson in his discussion o
---
 Yoruba sculpture (1971, 374–81).
The concept o
---
 jijora—mimesis at the midpoint—implies the work should
exist somewhere between complete abstraction and individual likeness.
It should resemble the individual and at the same time embody all the
ideal Yoruba characteristics without overemphasizing any one. The extreme stylization o
---
 pose and 
---
acial expression o
---
 the traditional 
---
ormal
portrait is an attempt to achieve this state by circumventing to a degree the
inherent speciﬁcity o
---
 the photographic portrait. Odo, depiction midway
between in
---
ancy and old age, at the prime o
---
 li
---
e, is seen in the strength
o
---
 the pose and in the 
---
acial expression, both o
---
 which seem to imply the
subject’s maturity and wisdom. I
---
arahon, ‘‘visibility,’’ implies clarity and deﬁnition o
---
 
---
orm and line, and a subsequent clarity o
---
 identity. This is emphasized in the photograph by the isolation o
---
 the subject against the neutral
background, in the sculptural dimensions and symmetry o
---
 the ﬁgure, and
in the inclusion o
---
 objects symbolizing the subject’s position in Yoruba
society.

---
ormal
portrait reveals two obvious similarities in 
---
orm: in both, the head invariably

viewed

The traditional 
---

---
unctioning
as an integral part o
---

---
or a signiﬁcant length o
---

meant to memorialize the subject not so much as a unique individual, as in
much British portraiture, but rather in terms o
---

traditional Yoruba ideals and 
---
ulﬁlled his given position in society. When
the subject dies, this portrait might be carried in his 
---
uneral procession to
particularize the ancestral Egungun (Schiltz 1978, 51). It is the portrait that
might be hung in his crypt or laminated to his tombstone, and published
in memoriam each year in the Lagos Daily Times.
The memorial pages o
---

---
estation in contemporary Nigerian Culture o
---

---
or, and veneration o
---

---

deceased, many o
---

Each memorial almost always includes a photograph and a brie
---

and description o
---

---

---

---
ten pictured in traditional dress in the traditional 
---
ormal pose, while Yoruba whose accomplishments could be identiﬁed with contemporary Nigerian culture were o
---
ten pictured in modern
dress in a variety o
---

social position, and education.8

The Yoruba photograph itsel
---

---

Family members, relatives,

---

and status. The photograph is sometimes believed to possess additional

---
ew

---

twins. Because twins are sacred children with connections to the spirit
world, it is especially important to show them proper respect. Photographs
are o

photographs o

complex when one twin dies be
---
ore their photograph is taken. I
---

were o
---

photographer prints this single negative twice, so that the twins appear to
be sitting side by side in the ﬁnal photograph (ﬁg. 8). I
---
 the twins were o
---

opposite sexes, the surviving twin is photographed once in male clothing
and once in 
---
emale clothing. Sometimes these two exposures are made on
separate negatives, which must then be printed together; and sometimes
they are made on opposite halves o
---

which can be printed without any darkroom manipulation (ﬁg. 9). In either
case the photographer attempts to conceal the line blending the two separate exposures in order to maintain the illusion o
---

in a single photograph.

---
orm regional unions, which meet about every six months.
The unions regulate such things as the price structure o
---
 the various types
o
---
 photographs and services, the details o
---
 apprenticeship, and the pro
---
essional conduct o
---
 its members.
The typical photography studio, such as those in Ila-Orangun, is usually
small but eﬃciently laid out. Double doors swing open to reveal to the
passerby samples o
---
 the photographer’s work (ﬁg. 11). A backdrop hangs
a 
---
ew 
---
eet inside the studio, with a bench 
---
or the sitter placed immediately in 
---
ront o
---
 it (see ﬁg. 7). These backdrops are painted by sign painters
in various shades o
---
 black, white, and gray. They o
---
ten show a 
---
ascinating
but naive use o
---
 Western perspective, and usually mix traditional and contemporary moti
---
s. Behind the backdrop is a tiny darkroom, o
---
ten without
either electricity or running water. Along one wall is a narrow table on
which are set the processing solutions in enameled bowls 
---
rom the market. A kerosene lantern with a red cloth surrounding the globe serves as
a sa
---
elight. An old postcard-size view camera is installed with its back to a
window 
---
or use as a solar enlarger; a mirror, located outside the window, is
256

Stephen Sprague

tilted to reﬂect the sunlight through the system. An enlargement is made
by placing a negative in the back o
---
 the camera and projecting its image
onto a sheet o
---
 photographic paper clipped to a vertical easel. Except 
---
or
minor variations, this makes up the photographer’s entire 
---
acilities.
The traditional view camera that takes postcard-size glass plates has been
increasingly relegated to the darkroom as an enlarger. Since about 1960
there has become available a wide variety o
---
 more ﬂexible, twin-lens reﬂex
cameras that take twelve two-inch square negatives on inexpensive 120 roll
ﬁlm. The success and status o
---
 a photographer are o
---
ten indicated by the
camera and other equipment he owns. A beginning photographer will own
a cheap plastic Russian Lubitel or Chinese Sea Gull; the majority own Japanese Yashicas, and a 
---
ew photographers in the large cities own expensive
German Rollicords or Rolliﬂexes.
From the two-inch square negative o
---
 these cameras, the photographers
oﬀer standardized photograph sizes based on the old British view camera
negative 
---
ormats. These are passport (2" × 2"), postcard (3" × 5"), hal
---
 plate
(3" × 4" ), and 
---
ull plate (6" × 8" ). The larger sizes o
---
 11" × 14" or 16" × 20" and
occasionally 20" × 30" con
---
orm to the sizes o
---
 photographic paper available.
Many photographers oﬀer additional services, including multipleprinting and hand coloring, and mounting techniques ranging 
---
rom
simple cardboard mounts to elaborate 
---
rames and 
---
reestanding cutouts.
Some photographers have a specialty; 
---
or example, Sir Special Photo o
---
 IlaOrangun is known 
---
or his skill in mounting a portrait behind a mirror. He
says the technique came to him in a dream. He scratches away the mirror
coating in the shape o
---
 the subject and then places the photograph behind
the mirror, allowing only the subject to show through.
I
---
 the customer does not like what the studio has to oﬀer, other 
---
rames
are available in the market and 
---
rom the sign painters. Sign painters make
very popular 
---
rames by painting a design and a proverb in English or
Yoruba on the back o
---
 a sheet o
---
 glass while leaving room 
---
or one or more
postcard-size photographs to show through.
This brie
---
 introduction to Yoruba photography suggests some broader
implications and questions.11 One o
---
 the ﬁrst questions that might be asked
is why certain groups in West A
---
rica, like the Yoruba, have integrated photography into their cultures, while other groups, such as the Hausa, appear
to make little use o
---
 the medium.12 It is suggested that societies with a
strong tradition o
---
 ﬁgurative art, such as the Yoruba, have aesthetic values
and a need 
---
or representation that could be satisﬁed and understood in
a photograph. For example, the Yoruba were initially introduced to the
Yoruba Photography

257

medium o
---
 photography by the British, whose photographic portraits appeared to display values important to the Yoruba. This made the use
---
ulness
o
---
 the photography immediately apparent. On the other hand, societies
such as the Hausa, which have a more abstract aesthetic tradition involving
decorative pattern and design, would have less use 
---
or, or even understanding o
---
, the photographic image. Also, any society that has a long history
o
---
 dominant Muslim inﬂuence, again the Hausa, would be less likely to
make use o
---
 the medium because o
---
 the strong Muslim prohibition against
graven images, which speciﬁcally includes photographs. Another important consideration is that a society with an economic system that could
accommodate the commissioning and production o
---
 photographs would
be more likely to make use o
---
 the medium. The Yoruba have a long history
o
---
 urbanization with a developed tradition o
---
 individual enterprise in the
production o
---
 goods and the marketing o
---
 specialized skills.
The large number o
---
 photographs available 
---
rom individual Yorubas
and 
---
rom photographers’ negative ﬁles 
---
orm a vast visual data bank that
is unique because it has been generated entirely by members o
---
 a nonWestern culture. This material might be utilized in a number o
---
 ways. The
most obvious would be simply to study the subject matter o
---
 photographs
available in a particular community in order to discover the existence o
---

people, ceremonies, events, and even objects and masquerades that otherwise might not be known. Copies o
---
 these photographs could be used to
elicit more in
---
ormation 
---
rom other members o
---
 the community.
More important, as this essay has shown, these photographs are ‘‘coded
in Yoruba’’ and can also give us much in
---
ormation about how the Yoruba
see themselves—about their cultural values and their view o
---
 the world.
But understanding all the implications o
---
 this wealth o
---
 visual in
---
ormation
will not be easy. A coherent methodology does not exist 
---
or extracting cultural and historical data 
---
rom even our own heritage o
---
 
---
amily snapshots
and anonymous photographs, and the 
---
ormulation o
---
 a methodology 
---
or
interpreting the photographic heritage o
---
 a non-Western society has never
been attempted as 
---
ar as I am aware. One hopes that continued investigation will eventually lead not only to a better knowledge o
---
 the Yoruba
view o
---
 themselves, but also to a better cross-cultural understanding o
---

how we communicate through mediated visual images o
---
 the world, and
to the 
---
ormulation o
---
 a methodology to deal with these images.

 Abiku means, literally, ‘‘we are born to die.’’ Children who are discovered to
be abiku must be paid special ritual attention in order to keep them in this
world; otherwise they will surely die and return to their spirit world. See
Mobolade 1973

ormation.

 Newspaper photographers in the cities have adapted a more candid journalistic approach, but their range o
---
 subject matter is much the same—predominantly people at ceremonial or other newsworthy events. News photographs typical o
---
 Western papers, such as accidents, disasters, or action
pictures o
---
 sports, seldom appear.
 I am particularly indebted to the thinking and research o
---
 Sol Worth, who in
his book Through Navajo Eyes (Worth and Adair 1972) has demonstrated that
members o
---
 a culture or subculture who learn to use a new medium o
---
 communication (in this case, ﬁlm) will produce work that is structured in part
by their own cultural values and by their culture’s perception o
---
 the world.
 This describes the ideal 
---
orm; sometimes the ﬁgure will be cut by the edge
o
---
 the 
---
rame, and occasionally the subject will be smiling. Although minor
variations are common, the traditional 
---
ormal portrait always maintains its
distinct identity.
 Many types o
---
 nineteenth- and early twentieth-century British photographic
portraits were looked at in detail. This included the work o
---

studio photographs (Hillier 1976), and colonial British photographs o
---
 India
(Worswick and Embree 1976).

---

rom the obituaries in the London Daily Times. But the emphasis on the photograph, the

---

a distinct 
---
orm.
Betty Wass (1975) has shown that Yoruba dress, as depicted in six hundred
photographs dating 
---
rom 1900 to 1974, does relate to the event photographed
and to the social position and education o
---
 the subject, and that the percentage o
---
 indigenous dress increases 
---
rom 1900 to 1974 as a 
---
unction o
---
 increased
national consciousness and pride.

michael aird runs his own publishing company, Keeaira Press, in Queensland,
Australia, specializing in books on photography and Aboriginal history. His most
recent publication is Brisbane Blacks (2001). Prior to his work as a publisher he
was a Curator in the Queensland Museum in Brisbane. He organized an exhibition o
---
